Chapter 3 · Sections 61–63

Application of Chapter 3

Chapter 3 of the BIF Act is the security of payment regime — payment claims, payment schedules, adjudication, suspension and the rest. Sections 61 to 63 set the outer fence: which contracts the Chapter catches, what happens if you also chase a subcontractor's charge under Chapter 4, and how the statutory rights sit alongside whatever the contract says.

Section 61 — Application of chapter

Section 61 is the gateway. It applies Chapter 3 broadly to construction contracts in Queensland, then carves out a small set of exceptions. If you can't fit a contract into one of the exceptions, assume Chapter 3 applies.

Section 61 BIF Act – Application of chapter
61 Application of chapter

(1)  Subject to subsections (2) to (4), this chapter applies to construction contracts—

(a)  whether written or oral, or partly written and partly oral; and

(b)  whether expressed to be governed by the law of Queensland or a jurisdiction other than Queensland; and

(c)  whether entered into before or after the commencement of this section, other than to the extent the repealed Building and Construction Industry Payments Act 2004 continues to apply to unfinished matters under section 205.

(2)  This chapter does not apply to—

(a)  a construction contract to the extent that it forms part of a loan agreement, a contract of guarantee or a contract of insurance under which a recognised financial institution undertakes—

(i)  to lend an amount or to repay an amount lent; or

(ii)  to guarantee payment of an amount owing or repayment of an amount lent; or

(iii)  to provide an indemnity relating to construction work carried out, or related goods and services supplied, under the construction contract; or

(b)  a construction contract for the carrying out of domestic building work if a resident owner is a party to the contract, to the extent the contract relates to a building or part of a building where the resident owner resides or intends to reside; or

(c)  a construction contract under which it is agreed that the consideration payable for construction work carried out under the contract, or for related goods and services supplied under the contract, is to be calculated other than by reference to the value of the work carried out or the value of the goods and services supplied.

(3)  This chapter does not apply to a construction contract to the extent it includes—

(a)  provisions under which a party undertakes to carry out construction work, or supply related goods and services in relation to construction work, as an employee of the party for whom the work is to be carried out or the related goods and services are to be supplied; or

(b)  provisions under which a party undertakes to carry out construction work, or to supply related goods and services in relation to construction work, as a condition of a loan agreement with a recognised financial institution; or

(c)  provisions under which a party undertakes—

(i)  to lend an amount or to repay an amount lent; or

(ii)  to guarantee payment of an amount owing or repayment of an amount lent; or

(iii)  to provide an indemnity relating to construction work carried out, or related goods and services supplied, under the construction contract.

(4)  This chapter does not apply to a construction contract to the extent it deals with construction work carried out outside Queensland or related goods and services supplied for construction work carried out outside Queensland.

(5)  In this section—

resident owner, in relation to a construction contract for carrying out domestic building work, means a resident owner under the Queensland Building and Construction Commission Act 1991, schedule 1B, section 1, but does not include a person—

(a)  who holds, or should hold, an owner-builder permit under the Queensland Building and Construction Commission Act 1991 relating to the work; or

(b)  who is a building contractor within the meaning of the Queensland Building and Construction Commission Act 1991.

Read this as a default-on switch. The Chapter applies to construction contracts whether they are written, oral or a mix; whether they are governed by Queensland law or some other law; and whether they were signed before or after Chapter 3 commenced. The choice-of-law clause your lawyers fought over does not get you out of the Queensland security of payment regime if the work is in Queensland.

The exceptions in s61(2)–(4) are narrow. The most relevant for a typical PM are: (a) parts of a contract that are really a financing arrangement with a bank; (b) genuine domestic building work where a resident owner is on the other side of the contract; (c) contracts where the price is not calculated by reference to the value of the work (for example, lump-sum profit-share or success-fee arrangements); (d) employment-style provisions; and (e) work physically located outside Queensland.

The onus sits with the party trying to escape the Act. If you are the respondent and you want to argue the contract is excluded, you have to prove it. Drafting the contract to say it is excluded does not make it so — the question is one of substance.

A "rough and ready" regime that demands strict compliance

The courts have repeatedly described the security of payment regime as a fast, informal, interim process — designed to keep cash moving down the chain while the parties' final legal rights are sorted out later. In Musico v Davenport [2003] NSWSC 977, McDougall J described the equivalent NSW Act as:

[107] a somewhat rough and ready way of assessing a builder's entitlement to progress claims. It may also be accepted that the procedure is intended not only to be swift, but also to be carried out with the minimum amount of formality and expense.

That informality is balanced by an unforgiving attitude to compliance. The Queensland authorities treat the statute as creating a parallel regime — statutory rights that sit alongside the contract (see Beckhaus v Brewarrina Council [2002] NSWSC 960, [60] (Macready AJ); Falgat Constructions Pty Ltd v Equity Australia Corp Pty Ltd [2005] NSWCA 49; (2005) 62 NSWLR 385) — but only for those who follow the statutory steps.

In F.K. Gardner & Sons Pty Ltd v Dimin Pty Ltd [2007] 1 Qd R 10; [2006] QSC 243, Lyons J put the point bluntly:

[24] The Act sets up a statutory regime for the recovery of progress claims and it is dependent on a series of steps being completed. There must be a valid statutory entitlement to a progress payment before a payment claim can be made and then if a payment schedule does not issue within time the unpaid portion of the claim becomes a debt. Such a statutory regime depends on strict compliance with the provisions in the Act.

The same theme runs through De Neefe Signs Pty Ltd v Build1 (Qld) Pty Ltd; Traffic Technologies Traffic Hire Pty Ltd v Build1 (Qld) Pty Ltd [2010] QSC 279 (Fryberg J), Tailored Projects Pty Ltd v Jedfire Pty Ltd [2009] 2 Qd R 171; [2009] QSC 32 (Douglas J at 176, [18]), and Simcorp Development and Constructions Pty Ltd v Gold Coast Titans Property Pty Ltd [2010] QSC 162, where Douglas J said at [26] that:

[26] Failure to adhere strictly to the statutory regime for the recovery of claims has been held to preclude reliance on the special statutory rights available under the Act.

The interstate position is the same. In Walter Construction Group Ltd v CPL (Surry Hills) Pty Ltd [2003] NSWSC 266 at [59], Nicholas J said the Act:

[59] has been said that the Act offers to a claimant special statutory rights which override general contractual rights and place the claimant in a privileged position (Jemzone Pty Ltd v Trytan Pty Ltd (2002) NSWSC 395 at para 41 per Austin J). In order to exercise these rights compliance with the requirements which establish them are necessary.

And in Taylor Projects Group Pty Ltd v Brick Dept Pty Ltd [2005] NSWSC 439, Einstein J said at [49]:

[49] In my view it is simply critical for a rigid approach to be taken to compliance with the terms of the Act, particularly for the reason that the legislation provides for a fast dual-track interim determination, reserving the parties' final legal entitlements for subsequent determination.

The Victorian Court of Appeal struck the same note in Saville v Hallmarc Construction Pty Ltd [2015] VSCA 318 (Warren CJ and Tate JA), drawing on Chase Oyster Bar Pty Ltd v Hamo Industries Pty Ltd [2010] NSWCA 190; (2010) 78 NSWLR 393:

The considerations identified by the New South Wales Court of Appeal, as described above, indicate that the strict observance of the procedural steps in the statutory scheme, including time limits, was mandated by the legislature as the price by which a claimant can take the benefit of the regime. The observance of certain procedural steps are essential to the assumption of jurisdiction by an adjudicator. An assessment of whether those procedural steps have been observed may require matters of evaluation that travel beyond a mechanical process.

Set against the strict-compliance line is a softer thread that says — especially for the content of payment claims — "reasonable" compliance can be enough: see T&M Buckley Pty Ltd v 57 Moss Rd Pty Ltd [2010] QCA 381, [33] (Philippides J; Fraser and White JJA agreeing); Nepean Engineering Pty Ltd v Total Process Services Pty Ltd (in liq) [2005] NSWCA 409 at [34] (Hodgson JA), [76] (Ipp JA). And in Hawkins Construction (Australia) Pty Ltd v Mac's Industrial Pipework Pty Ltd [2002] NSWCA 136, Davies AJA warned that the equivalent provisions:

should not be approached in an unduly technical manner… The terms used by subs (2) of s 13 are well understood words of the English language. They should be given their normal and natural meaning. As the words are used in relation to events occurring in the construction industry, they should be applied in a common sense practical manner.

See also Amasya Enterprises Pty Ltd & Anor v Asta Developments (Aust) Pty Ltd & Anor (No 2) [2015] VSC 500, [87] (Vickery J), citing Protectavale Pty Ltd v K2K Pty Ltd [2008] FCA 1248, [10]–[11] (Finkelstein J). The practical takeaway: timing rules and jurisdictional steps are unforgiving; the form and content of payment claims are read commercially. Treat the procedural deadlines (when to serve, when to respond, when to apply for adjudication) as bright lines.

One recent illustration of the commercial reading: in Hawkesbury City Council v The Civil Experts Pty Ltd trading as TCE Contracting [2023] NSWSC 962, Ball J held that where a contract said "Cost + 25%" without defining "cost", the adjudicator was entitled to read "cost" as covering all costs incurred by the claimant in carrying out its construction business, including off-site overheads.

The s61(2) exceptions — onus is on the party trying to escape the Act

Section 61(1) is a statement of general application; s61(2) carves out exceptions. The party that wants to argue an exception applies bears the onus of proof. As Nicholas J explained in Walter Construction Group Limited v CPL (Surry Hills) Pty Ltd [2003] NSWSC 266 in relation to the analogous NSW provision:

It seems clear to me that the intention of the legislature is that the Act applies to any construction contract, s 7(1) being a statement of general application. It is also clear that its intention is that the classes of contract described in s 7(2) are exceptions to the general rule so that if a party wishes to avoid its application it must demonstrate that the contract is within an excepted class. Obviously, it is probable that the features of a contract which might bring it within an excepted class will be within the knowledge of the party seeking to avoid the Act. Consistent with the principles referred to, that circumstance is further indication of the intention that the onus of proof concerning the exception is on the party claiming that the contract is within it.

"Forms part of" a loan agreement — s61(2)(a)

The exception only bites where the construction contract is genuinely included in the financing arrangement, not merely associated with it. In Consolidated Constructions Pty Ltd v Ettamogah Pub [2004] NSWSC 110, McDougall J considered the words "forms part of":

As a matter of ordinary English usage, something may be said to "form part of" another if the first thing is included or incorporated within the second. The first thing may form part of the second as a result of some natural process or as a result of some artificial process (for example, a process of manufacture). In general terms, the words "forms part of" seem to me to connote something akin to inclusion, as opposed to association. In a particular case, however, it may be difficult to discern the point at which association changes to inclusion: that is to say, the point at which one thing may be said to form part of, rather than merely to be associated with, another.

…

In both ordinary English usage and legal usage, the words "forms part of" therefore seem to indicate a relationship that is more than ancillary or associative. It is not enough to say only that the two things in question are in some way connected, for example because the one bears in some way on the other. The point at which connection becomes inclusion – at which the ancillary becomes integral – may not be easy to discern, and will in any event depend upon the facts of the particular case and the terms of the particular documents.

His Honour held at [21] that a "construction contract will not form part of a loan agreement unless, in some way, the former is included in, or incorporated into, the latter". A separately negotiated head contract that simply sits next to a project finance facility will not be excluded just because the bank's cash funds the build.

"Indemnity relating to construction work" — s61(2)(a)(iii)

Watch this exclusion carefully if you are the claimant and your claim is dressed up as an indemnity rather than as a claim for work done. In Forte Sydney Carlingford Pty Ltd v Li [2022] FCA 1499, Stewart J granted an injunction restraining the claimant from re-submitting an adjudication application after two earlier attempts. The claimant's payment claim was based entirely on an indemnity under the construction contract rather than on construction works carried out. Stewart J held that s 7(3)(c)(iii) of the NSW SOP Act — the equivalent of s61(3)(c)(iii) of the BIF Act — operates to exclude any part of a payment claim that falls under such an indemnity from adjudication, with the result that the payment claim itself was invalid.

"Domestic building work" and resident owners — s61(2)(b)

Chapter 3 does not apply to a construction contract for domestic building work where a "resident owner" is on the other side — but the definition of resident owner in s61(5) excludes anyone who holds (or should hold) an owner-builder permit under the QBCC Act, or who is a building contractor under that Act. So a tradie building their own home in their own name is not necessarily out of reach.

The Court of Appeal considered "resident owner" under the BCIPA equivalent in R J Neller Building Pty Ltd v Ainsworth [2008] QCA 397; [2009] 1 Qd R 390. Keane JA rejected the argument that an owner could only have one residence, holding:

In my respectful opinion, it is not possible to detect in the provisions of the BCIP Act an intention on the part of the legislature that the concept of "resident owner" should be confined in the way urged by Neller. The evident concern of the legislature in excluding contracts between builders and resident owners from the scope of the BCIP Act is to ensure that the ultimate consumers of the goods and services provided by the building and construction industry should not be subject to a regime which, among other things, imposes certain financial risks upon building owners who are participants in that industry to the advantage of the builders who contract with them. I shall discuss the effect of the BCIP Act upon the allocation of these financial risks in due course, but for the moment it is sufficient to say that, this being the concern which can be seen to inform the exclusion of "resident owners" from the scope of the legislation, there is little reason to think that it was any part of the legislative intention to alter the usual understanding that a person may be a resident of several locations at the same time.

The "building contractor" carve-out from the resident-owner definition was tested in Vis Constructions Ltd & Anor v Cockburn & Anor [2006] QSC 416. A husband and wife jointly owned land and engaged a builder to construct their home; the husband held a plastering licence but the wife did not. The adjudicator concluded that they were still a "resident owner". On review, Jones J said the decision "might well be wrong in law" but held that it was a non-jurisdictional error of law and so not reviewable.

For completeness, in BWAY Group Pty v Pasiopoulos [2019] VCC 691, Marks J held that whether a party to a domestic building contract is "in the business of building residences" for the purposes of the equivalent Victorian provision turns on objective indicia such as commercial history — not the parties' subjective intentions.

Consideration "calculated other than by reference to" the value of the work — s61(2)(c)

This exception targets contracts where the consideration is fixed by something other than the value of the work done or goods supplied — for example, contracts that pay a percentage tied to a downstream sale, or fixed fees that are not calibrated to work value. Einstein J in Brambles Australia Limited v Philip Davenport & Ors [2004] NSWSC 120 explained the purpose of the equivalent NSW exclusion:

"Clearly enough, this sub-section serves to exclude certain construction contracts from the reach of the Act. The construction contracts so excluded are identified by reference to whether or not the consideration payable for construction work carried out under the contract or for related goods and services supplied under the contract is to be calculated."

His Honour adopted Nicholas J's approach in Walter Construction Group Limited v CPL (Surry Hills) Pty Ltd [2003] NSWSC 266. In Sheppard Homes Pty Ltd v FADL Industrial Pty Ltd [2010] QSC 228, the amounts payable under the contract were determined by reference to what the owner paid the builder, not the value of the consultant's services. Fryberg J held that the equivalent BCIPA exclusion (s 3(2)(c)) applied — so the contract between the builder and the consultant was outside the Act.

"To guarantee payment" — s61(3)(c)

An undertaking to pay money owed will fall within the guarantee exclusion. In Walton Construction (Qld) Pty Ltd v Robert Salce & Ors [2008] QSC 235, an undertaking to pay money owed was held to fall clearly within the exclusion under the equivalent BCIPA provision (s 3(3)(c)(ii)).

Work or goods outside Queensland — s61(4)

Section 61(4) gives the Act a territorial limit. It excludes the Act to the extent the contract deals with construction work carried out outside Queensland (or related goods and services supplied for such work). Philip McMurdo J explained the rationale in Wiggins Island Coal Export Terminal Pty Ltd v Monadelphous Engineering Pty Ltd & Ors [2015] QSC 307:

"The evident purpose of s 3(4) is to confine the operation of the Act to circumstances which have a relevant association with Queensland. It is of the essence of construction work, as defined, that it involves work in relation to certain land. It is therefore logical that the Act would be given a territorial limitation which is defined according to whether the relevant land is in Queensland."

The location of the ultimate works matters more than where each fabrication step happens. In the same case, a shiploader and "tripper" were fabricated in Malaysia for installation at a coal terminal near Gladstone. The respondent argued that a payment claim made about four weeks before the shiploader arrived in Queensland was outside the Act. Philip McMurdo J disagreed:

The work of the fabrication of this equipment was carried out outside Queensland. But that work was construction work only because it was an integral part of construction work undertaken inside Queensland. In my view, that fabrication could not be relevantly characterised both as construction work carried out outside Queensland and an integral part of construction work carried out within Queensland.

In requiring the fabrications of this equipment, the contract was providing for part of the construction of a structure in Queensland and was, in the sense of s 3(4), "dealing with" construction work carried out in Queensland. It follows that the application of the Act was not displaced by s 3(4).

The Victorian County Court took a similar line in South City Plaster Pty Ltd v Modscape Pty Ltd [2018] VCC 1576, where Macnamara J held that the VIC Act applied even though some work was carried out in Tasmania, because the contract did not propose work outside Victoria and the Tasmanian work was "not an integral part of construction work."

Cross-border projects were addressed directly in Lendlease Building Pty Ltd v BCS Airport Systems Pty Ltd & Ors [2024] QSC 164. The Gold Coast Airport site straddles the Queensland–NSW border, and some of the works were carried out in NSW. Lendlease tried to quash an adjudication on the basis that the payment claim was invalid because part of the work was in NSW. Sullivan J rejected the argument: s61(4) only displaces the Act where construction work is wholly outside Queensland; what matters is the location of the ultimate building, structure or works, not where each individual activity takes place; and a payment claim is not invalidated merely because it includes a component of "excluded construction work". The cross-border claim was valid.

Final payments

Final payments fall within Chapter 3. The definitions of "final payment" (s75(3)) and "progress payment" (s64) confirm that the Chapter is not limited to interim claims — the final claim under a contract is also a payment claim for the purposes of the Act.

Section 62 — Effect of giving notice of claim for subcontractors' charges

Section 62 governs what happens when a claimant tries to run two parallel processes — a Chapter 3 payment claim and a Chapter 4 subcontractors' charge — for the same work. The short version: pick one. Lodging a Chapter 4 notice of claim shuts down Chapter 3 for the same work.

Section 62 BIF Act – Effect of giving notice of claim for subcontractors' charges
62 Effect of giving notice of claim for subcontractors' charges

(1)  This section applies if a person gives a notice of claim under chapter 4 in relation to construction work or related goods and services the subject of a construction contract.

(2)  Proceedings or other action may not be started or continued by the person under part 3 for all or part of the construction work or related goods and services.

(3)  Without limiting subsection (2), if the person gave a respondent a payment claim for all or part of the construction work or related goods and services before or at the same time as giving the notice of claim—

(a)  the respondent is not required to pay an amount to the person under section 77(2) in relation to the claim; and

(b)  amounts may not be recovered by the person as a debt owing to the person in any court of competent jurisdiction in relation to the claim; and

(c)  if the person made an adjudication application in relation to the claim and the application has not been decided by an adjudicator before the notice of claim is given, the person is taken to have withdrawn the application; and

(d)  if the person made an adjudication application in relation to the claim and the application has been decided by an adjudicator before the notice of claim was given—

(i)  the respondent to the application is not required to pay the adjudicated amount under section 90; and

(ii)  the registrar must not give the person an adjudication certificate under section 91 relating to the adjudication; and

(iii)  any adjudication certificate provided in relation to the adjudication can not be enforced by the person under section 93 as a judgment of a court; and

(e)  the person may not suspend, or continue to suspend, carrying out all or part of the construction work or the supply of the related goods and services under section 98.

(4)  This section does not affect the operation of section 95 and an adjudication application taken to have been withdrawn by the person under subsection (3)(c) is taken to have been withdrawn for the purpose of section 95.

(5)  This section does not stop the person serving, under this chapter, a payment claim in relation to all or part of the construction work or related goods and services and taking other action under this chapter in relation to that claim, if the notice of claim in so far as it relates to the construction work or related goods and services, or part, is withdrawn.

(6)  In this section—

notice of claim see section 122(1).

If you (or a subcontractor you are dealing with) issue a Chapter 4 notice of claim of charge over the same work, the Chapter 3 process for that work freezes. You can't start or continue court proceedings under Part 3 for it. If you had already served a payment claim, the respondent doesn't have to pay under s77(2), you can't recover the unpaid amount as a debt, any pending adjudication is treated as withdrawn, and any decided adjudication can't be enforced. You also lose the right to suspend work under s98 for that scope.

The freeze isn't permanent. Section 62(5) lets you come back to Chapter 3 if the notice of claim is withdrawn — but the withdrawal needs to be effective. In Heavy Plant Leasing Pty Ltd v McConnell Dowell Constructors (Aust) Pty Ltd & Ors [2013] QCA 386, the Court of Appeal observed (under the predecessor BCIPA provision) that it is implicit that the notice of claim must be valid in the first place, and that a "withdrawn" notice must be withdrawn in a way that is effective in respect of all relevant parties — regardless of whether the withdrawal is in the approved form.

Practically: don't run both regimes for the same scope at the same time. Choose your weapon early. If you fire off a notice of claim of charge while an adjudication is on foot, you will be taken to have withdrawn the adjudication application — which has knock-on consequences under s95 for any later application.

Section 63 — Act does not limit claimant's other rights

Section 63 makes clear that Chapter 3 layers on top of the contract; it doesn't replace it. The statutory entitlements are additional, not substitutional.

Section 63 BIF Act – Act does not limit claimant's other rights
63 Act does not limit claimant other rights

A claimant's entitlements and remedies under this chapter do not limit—

(a)  another entitlement a claimant may have under a construction contract; or

(b)  any remedy a claimant may have for recovering the other entitlement.

Whatever rights a claimant has under the contract — to claim payment, to charge interest, to call on security, to pursue damages, to refer disputes to arbitration — remain available. Chapter 3 sits alongside them. As Austin J observed in Jemzone Pty Ltd v Trytan Pty Ltd [2002] NSWSC 395 in relation to the structure of the equivalent NSW Act:

generally leaves it to the construction contract to define the rights of the parties but makes "default provisions" to fill in the contractual gaps.

For PMs this matters in two practical ways. First, an adjudicator's decision is interim — it determines who holds the cash now, not who is ultimately entitled to it. The contract's final-account, dispute-resolution and damages mechanisms still run their course. Second, electing to use Chapter 3 does not waive your contractual rights; you can run an adjudication and still pursue the contractual claim later for any shortfall.

Practice tips

  • Assume Chapter 3 applies to any construction contract you are running in Queensland — whether written, oral, pre-existing or governed by another state's law — until you have specifically ruled out an exception in s61(2), (3) or (4).
  • If you are the respondent and you want to argue a contract is excluded, remember the onus is on you. Build the evidence (financing structure, residency, pricing mechanism, work location) early — don't rely on the contract simply saying it is excluded.
  • For cross-border or partly off-shore work, the question is the location of the ultimate works, not where each fabrication or activity happens. After Lendlease v BCS Airport Systems, a payment claim that includes some out-of-state work is not automatically invalid — s61(4) only bites where the work is wholly outside Queensland.
  • Treat the procedural steps and timeframes as bright lines. The form and content of payment claims are read commercially, but timing and jurisdictional steps are unforgiving — miss a deadline and you forfeit the statutory right.
  • Don't run a Chapter 3 payment claim and a Chapter 4 notice of claim of charge for the same scope at the same time. Lodging the Chapter 4 notice freezes Chapter 3 for that work, withdraws any pending adjudication, and stops you enforcing any adjudicated amount or suspending under s98.
  • Remember that an adjudication outcome is interim. Section 63 preserves your contractual rights — final account, damages, security, dispute resolution — so plan the litigation and contract administration strategy together, not separately.

Chapter 3 · Section 64

Construction contract

The BIF Act’s payment regime only switches on if there is a “construction contract” between the parties. Section 64 of the BIF Act defines that term broadly — it is not limited to a signed, formal agreement. Getting this threshold question right matters: if there is no construction contract (or no “other arrangement”), an adjudicator has no jurisdiction and any decision will be set aside.

Section 64 BIF Act – “construction contract” (definition)
64 Definitions for chapter

In this chapter—

construction contract means a contract, agreement or other arrangement under which 1 party undertakes to carry out construction work for, or to supply related goods and services to, another party.

“Contract”, “agreement” and “other arrangement”

The definition deliberately separates a “contract”, an “agreement” and an “other arrangement”. That structure suggests “other arrangement” reaches further than a common law contract. That was the view of Nicholas J in Okaroo Pty Ltd v Vos Constructions and Joinery Pty Ltd [2005] NSWSC 45:

With regard to the authorities, and to its context in the Act, in my opinion the term “arrangement” in the definition is a wide one, and encompasses transactions or relationships which are not legally enforceable agreements. The distinction in the definition between “a contract” and “other arrangement” is intended by the legislature to be one of substance so that under the Act construction contracts include agreements which are legally enforceable and transactions which are not. Thus in distinguishing between these relationships I understand the legislature intends that “contract” is to be given its common law meaning and that “arrangement” means a transaction or relationship which is not enforceable at law as a contract would be. Accordingly I reject the submission for Okaroo that the term “arrangement” should be understood to mean an agreement which is tantamount to a contract enforceable at law.

Douglas J approved this approach in Queensland in Bezzina Developers Pty Ltd v Deemah Stone (Qld) Pty Ltd [2007] QSC 286.

McDougall J explored the same idea in Machkevitch v Andrew Building Commissions [2012] NSWSC 546:

As a matter of language, it seems to be clear that the legislature intended that a "construction contract" could include both a "contract" (as that concept is known to and understood in the law) and some "other arrangements" that would not in law be regarded as contracts.

It seems to me, as a simple matter of reading the legislative words, that the concept of "other arrangement" is something which goes beyond the concept of "contract".

No doubt, the legislature had in mind that, from time to time, work would be done pursuant to arrangements which might not be susceptible to classification as contracts, formal or informal. Clearly, it did not intend that the entitlement to payment should depend on the degree of formality in the arrangements pursuant to which work should be done. In this respect, the legislative intention could be contrasted with the intention underlying s 10 of the Home Building Act 1989 (NSW), under which a builder is not entitled to enforce a contract unless it is licensed, and carries out work pursuant to a written contract.

The word "arrangement" may be thought to be a somewhat strange one in the context of the Act. It its primary meaning, it denotes the ordering or disposition of things (see, for example, the online editions of the Macquarie Dictionary and the Oxford English Dictionary). But the same reference sources suggest that a secondary meaning of "arrangement" denotes measures or preparations, or plans for the accomplishment of some purpose.

How much is enough to amount to an “other arrangement”?

Even though “other arrangement” is broader than a contract, the courts have set a floor: there must still be a settled, two-sided dealing — not just one party turning up and doing work.

In Crown Green Square Pty Ltd v Transport for NSW [2021] NSWSC 1557, Henry J refused progress payment relief because the claimant was not engaged by the respondent under a construction contract or “other arrangement”. The respondent had contracted with a related entity of the claimant, not the claimant itself. After the respondent failed to provide a payment schedule, the claimant sued, arguing the entitlement arose under an “other arrangement”. Henry J disagreed: the work obligation sat under a different construction contract to which the claimant was not a party. In obiter, her Honour said an “other arrangement” need not be legally binding but requires more than one party simply undertaking to do construction work for another — there “must be a concluded state of affairs between two or more parties involving some element of reciprocity or acceptance of mutual rights and obligations relating to payment or price for the works (which may or may not be legally binding obligations) under which one party undertakes to carry out construction work for another party to the arrangement.”

In Ventia Australia Pty Ltd v BSA Advanced Property Solutions (Fire) Pty Ltd [2021] NSWSC 1534, Rees J observed in obiter that “arrangement” is the right label where the parties’ dealings about the work lack the precision of express contractual terms. On the facts, the contract expressly provided that each work order created a new construction contract, and her Honour held the court could not look past that express regime to find an “arrangement”.

In Bettar Holdings Pty Ltd v RWC Brookvale Investments Pty Ltd, Cole DCJ held there was no construction contract or “other arrangement” where negotiations remained “subject to a comprehensive agreement being arrived at and recorded in a written and executed contract” that never materialised. Citing BSA Advanced Property Solutions (Fire) Pty Ltd v Ventia Australia Pty Ltd (2022) 108 NSWLR 350, his Honour confirmed that an “other arrangement” must involve an undertaking to perform work for consideration.

In Justar Property Group Pty Ltd v Chase Building Group (Canberra) Pty Ltd [2020] ACTSC 231, Mossop J had to decide whether the adjudicator had jurisdiction. The respondent argued the contract was actually with another company in its group. Mossop J held the adjudicator was entitled to find some “other arrangement” existed between the named claimant and respondent, and there was no error of law on the face of the determination. His Honour applied Machkevitch and looked for “a concluded state of affairs, which is bilateral at least, which can amount to an arrangement under which one party to it undertakes to perform construction work for another party to it”.

His Honour also adopted Nicholas J’s reasoning from Okaroo, that an “arrangement encompasses transactions or relationships which are not legally enforceable”, including the following passage:

the only matter for consideration is whether it is one under which one party undertakes to carry out construction work, or to supply related goods and services, for another party. There is no other requirement or qualification which is expressly or by implication included in the definition which must be satisfied. It may be safely assumed that had the legislature intended any additional requirement or qualification it would have included it in the definition.

And on what “arrangement” itself requires, McDougall J in Machkevitch said:

In my view, what is required is that there be something more than a mere undertaking; or something which can be said to give rise to an engagement, although not a legally enforceable engagement, between two parties; or a state of affairs under which one party undertakes to the other to do something; or an arrangement between parties to like effect.

In those circumstances, the court must look for a concluded state of affairs, which is bilateral at least, which can amount to an arrangement under which one party to it undertakes to perform construction work for another party to it. It is not necessary that the arrangement be legally enforceable; but an "arrangement" which is legally enforceable may be, a priori, a construction contract.

Identifying the right parties

Naming the wrong respondent is fatal. In Ratcliffe v Horizon Glass & Aluminium Pty Ltd [2023] NSWSC 196, Rees J set aside an adjudication determination because the claimant had named the principal’s individual director as the respondent, when the contract was actually with the principal company. Her Honour applied Brereton JA’s summary in Mills v Walsh [2022] NSWCA 255 at [73] on identifying parties:

“The parties to a contract are identified according to the objective theory of contract, which involves ascertaining the intention of the parties from their communications and the circumstances in their mutual knowledge, including their evident commercial aims and expectations; their subjective beliefs and intentions are irrelevant, save insofar as they are manifest and shared. However, the post-contractual conduct of the parties may more readily be resorted to for this purpose than for the purpose of construing contractual terms.”

On the balance of probabilities the parties had intended the principal company — not its director — to enter the contract, so the determination was set aside.

Queensland application: GCB Constructions

In GCB Constructions Pty Ltd v SEQ Formwork Pty Ltd & Ors [2023] QSC 71, the Queensland Supreme Court applied the “other arrangement” concept directly under s 64 BIF Act. The parties had had an on-site discussion about additional works. The adjudicator treated that conversation as an “other arrangement”.

Burns J held that to be an “other arrangement” under s 64 there must be at least a bilateral concluded state of affairs, and a sufficient degree of mutuality — including enough settled detail for the work or materials to be claimed with precision. The site discussion fell short for these reasons:

  • The payment claim was for the supply of material, but nothing in the discussion addressed supply of, or payment for, material.
  • There was no specification of the rates to be charged for labour.
  • Nothing was agreed as to the time for performance for any of the work, the making of the claim, the time for payment or retention money.

Because there was no construction contract or other arrangement, the adjudicator’s decision was void.

Pleading the arrangement and the “undertaking”

In EnerMech Pty Ltd v Acciona Infrastructure Projects Australia Pty Ltd [2024] NSWCA 162, Basten AJA (Meagher JA and Griffiths AJA agreeing), considering the equivalent NSW definitions, said at [16]:

“Two points are immediately apparent from these provisions: first, the Security of Payment Act does not purport to limit the amount or nature of a payment to which a party is entitled under a construction contract; secondly, there is a risk in compartmentalising payments according to their character, regardless of the terms of the contract, so as to contend that some fall within the concept of a progress payment for which a claim may be made, and some do not”.

In Class Electrical Services v Go Electrical [2013] NSWSC 363, McDougall J clarified his earlier remarks in Machkevitch on the word “undertaking”:

The first use of the word "undertaking" seems to me now to be somewhat unfortunate, having regard to the definition of "construction contract". It was intended to pick up undertakings of the kind said to have been given by Mr Machkevitch to the builder, as I explained above. It was not intended to be "an undertaking" in a cognate sense to the verb "undertakes" as it is used in the definition of "construction contract".

Thus, properly understood, I do not think that anything that I said in Machkevitch focused on what is required to satisfy or demonstrate the concept of undertaking to do construction work or supply related goods and services. It was concerned with the existence of a contract or arrangement.

Most recently, in Bettar Holdings Pty Ltd trading as Hunt Collaborative v RWC Brookvale Investment Pty Ltd as trustee for Brookvale Development Trust [2025] NSWCA 242, McHugh JA (Bell CJ and Kirk JA agreeing) confirmed that an “other arrangement” under the equivalent NSW definition must be properly pleaded with the material facts constituting the arrangement and the basis on which the party had “undertaken” to carry out work [135]–[136]. It also requires either a legally binding obligation or a “concluded state of affairs” involving “some element of reciprocity or acceptance of mutual rights and obligations relating to payment or price for the works” [139]. His Honour noted the conflict between Lendlease Engineering Pty Ltd v Timecon Pty Ltd and Crown Green Square Pty Ltd v Transport for NSW [140] but declined to resolve it — on the facts there was no “other arrangement” under either test, because the pleading only alleged a common law contract or an estoppel preventing denial of one, and there was no allegation that Hunt had “undertaken” to perform work on any other basis. Any quantum meruit case was foreclosed by the pleaded contract date of 27 October 2023, with no request for work or performance before then [141]–[142].

By contrast, in Bloc Constructions (NSW) Pty Limited v Alorra Piling (NSW) Pty Ltd [2025] NSWSC 1324, Peden J confirmed that no formal, signed contract is needed. At [51], her Honour held that even without a provable formal contract, the parties’ objective conduct — the principal requesting piling work, the contractor performing it, and the principal making payments — was enough to establish an “arrangement”. The decision underscores that the Act’s remedial purpose lets it operate on the commercial reality of work performed, even where strict legal enforceability might be open to question (the claimant would in any event likely have a restitutionary entitlement).

Both Okaroo and Machkevitch on “arrangement” were summarised by Stevenson J in IWN No 2 Pty Ltd v Level Orange Pty Ltd [2012] NSWSC 1439, at [25]. See also Vis Constructions Ltd & Anor v Cockburn & Anor [2006] QSC 416, where Jones J considered that to establish a contract, agreement or other arrangement within the meaning of Schedule 2 BCIPA, “[i]t must be established that there was an undertaking by one party…to carry out construction work or supply related goods to another party”.

Worked examples

  • Reference to an earlier contract is just background. Kaycee Trucking Pty Ltd v M and C Rogers Transport Pty Ltd & Ors [2014] QSC 185. There were two contracts between the parties — a 2012 contract and a 2013 contract. The payment claim mentioned both. The respondent argued the claim was therefore made under two contracts and invalid. Because no work claimed was actually done under the 2012 contract, the Court held the references to 2012 were background only and the claim was made under the 2013 agreement.
  • Email exchange as a “concluded state of affairs”. SMLXL Projects Pty Ltd v RIIS Retail A/S [2017] NSWDC 131. Dicker SC DCJ found that an email from the claimant setting out services performed, followed by an email from the respondent accepting the works, was enough to constitute a construction contract under the NSW Act — the works were agreed and amounted to “a concluded state of affairs”.
  • Whether an “other arrangement” must be legally binding. Lendlease Engineering Pty Ltd v Timecon Pty Ltd [2019] NSWSC 685. Ball J held that for an “other arrangement” to qualify under the NSW equivalent provision, it must give rise to a legally binding obligation, though not necessarily contractual in nature. Lendlease had argued there was no “contract or other arrangement” at all, so Timecon could not claim a progress payment. The judgment expressly distinguished earlier authority that an “other arrangement” need not be legally enforceable.

The Queensland approach to construction: Capricorn Quarries

In Capricorn Quarries Pty Ltd v Inline Communication Construction Pty Ltd [2012] QSC 388; [2013] 2 Qd R 1, Jackson J rejected the argument that BCIPA should be given a “remedial” construction. Instead, his Honour said the definitions of “construction contract”, “construction work” and “related goods and services” should be read “naturally”:

Having regard to the context as discussed, in my view, a “natural” construction of the relevant definitions of BCIPA in this case is to be preferred to an approach which seeks to extend the operation of BCIPA by a “liberal interpretation” to be engaged in with the purpose of increasing the width of the class of persons who are entitled to the benefit of a payment claim and correspondingly increasing the width of the class of persons who are subject to BCIPA’s restriction and obligations.

The language chosen by Parliament to define “construction contract”, “construction work” and “related goods and services” has no purpose other than to draw the line between who is in and who is out of those classes. There seems to be little logic in seeking to stretch that language either way. In saying this, I take a “natural” construction to be that arrived at by the usual process of the application of the common law of statutory interpretation, as affected by statute, but without a presumptive approach.

“Mixed” contracts

A construction contract can include both work that is “construction work” and work that is not. McDougall J in Brian Leigh Smith & Anor v Coastivity Pty Ltd [2008] NSWSC 313 held (at [35]):

The definition of “construction contract” does not require that all the work undertaken to be performed, or all the goods and services undertaken to be provided, pursuant to the contract should be construction work or related goods and services as defined. It is sufficient if some of them fall within the definition. Thus, it is not to the point that some of the obligations undertaken by Coastivity (for example, in relation to raising finance) do not amount to related services. The question must be whether any of the other obligations undertaken fall within the statutory definition.

The Queensland Court of Appeal took the same view in Thiess Pty Ltd v Warren Brothers Earthmoving Pty Ltd & Anor [2012] QCA 276, with Philippides J stating (at [56]):

In my view, his Honour was clearly correct in stating at [77] that “a contract or arrangement is a construction contract if it contains an undertaking of the type specified in the definition of construction contract, notwithstanding that it contains other undertakings or imposes other obligations not within the definition”. It also accords with the approach taken in New South Wales in respect of equivalent legislation: see Brian Leigh Smith v Coastivity Pty Ltd [2008] NSWSC 313 at [35]; HM Australia Holdings Pty Ltd v Edelbrand Pty Ltd [2011] NSWSC 604 at [30]. What is required for the purposes of the definition of “construction contract” is that it is one under which a party undertakes to carry out some “construction work”.

The same case confirmed that “the adjudicator’s determination as to the extent and value of the ‘construction work’ or ‘related goods and services’ the subject of the payment claim does not…concern a matter of jurisdictional fact”: Thiess Pty Ltd v Warren Brothers Earthmoving Pty Ltd & Anor [2012] QCA 276, [103] (Philippides J, Holmes and White JJA agreeing).

One claim, one contract

A payment claim must relate to one construction contract only, and “there can only be one adjudication application for any particular payment claim for any particular contract”: Matrix Projects (Qld) Pty Ltd v Luscombe [2013] QSC 4, [17]–[18] (Douglas J); Rail Corporation of NSW v Nebax Constructions [2012] NSWSC 6, [44] (McDougall J).

  • Claim spanning two contracts is invalid. Matrix Projects (Qld) Pty Ltd v Luscombe [2013] QSC 4. Work was performed under a period subcontract and separately as “do and charge” rectification. The Court rejected the argument that “arrangement” was wide enough to bundle both, holding the claim was not made under a single construction contract — fatal to its validity.
  • Adjudication void if the claim spans two contracts. Trinco (NSW) Pty Ltd v Alpha A Group Pty Ltd [2018] NSWSC 239. McDougall J held the adjudication determination void on reference-date grounds, but added that, if his contract construction was wrong, the determination would also be void because the payment claim related to works under both the first and second contract.

Side deeds

A side deed that varies the head contract is itself a construction contract. In APN DF2 Project 2 Pty Ltd v Grocon Constructors (Victoria) Pty Ltd [2014] VSC 596, a side deed was entered after the design and construct contract was signed, varying that contract. Vickery J held the side deed to be a “construction contract” under the Victorian Act:

It is plainly obvious that the concept of “construction contract” as defined in s 4 of the Act, encompasses both an original contract entered into between the parties (in this case the D&C Contract) and any instrument which validly effects a variation to the original contract (in this case the Side Deed). The Act could not properly operate in a commercial context if this was not the case.

In this case the Side Deed in law varied the D & C Contract, including the payment regime in critical respects. Further, the D & C Contract remained in place following entry into the Side Deed, subject to the alterations reflected in the variations. This resulted in the parties making two contracts which co-existed: the D & C Contract as amended by the Side Deed; and the Side Deed itself.

Practice tips

  • Before issuing or responding to a payment claim, identify the exact contract or arrangement it relies on — and the exact legal entities on each side. Naming the wrong respondent (a director instead of the company, or a related entity instead of the actual contracting party) will sink the claim: see Ratcliffe and Crown Green Square.
  • If you are working under an informal arrangement (verbal scope, site-meeting agreement, work-on-handshake), make sure the dealings cover the basics — what is being supplied, the rates, timing, payment terms. GCB Constructions shows that a vague on-site discussion is not enough to be an “other arrangement” under s 64.
  • Always issue one payment claim per construction contract. If work has been done under both a head subcontract and a separate “do and charge” arrangement, claim them separately (Matrix Projects; Trinco).
  • Treat a side deed or variation deed as a construction contract in its own right. If the side deed reorganises the payment regime, your claim regime needs to follow the deed (APN DF2).
  • Don’t panic if the contract bundles construction and non-construction obligations (e.g. finance, advisory work). The contract is still a construction contract so long as some of the obligations fall within the definition (Coastivity; Thiess).
  • Where there is no signed contract but the principal has been requesting and paying for the work, the conduct alone may amount to a construction contract (Bloc Constructions) — document that conduct contemporaneously (emails accepting works, scope confirmations, payment history) so the “concluded state of affairs” is provable.

Chapter 3 · Sections 65–66

Construction work & related goods and services

Whether the BIF Act covers your contract turns on two definitions: is the work “construction work” (s 65) or, if not, is what is being supplied “related goods and services” (s 66)? A single contract can mix both, and it can also include scope that is neither — in which case only the construction-work and related-goods-and-services portions attract the Act’s payment regime.

Section 65 – Meaning of construction work

Section 65 BIF Act – Meaning of construction work
65 Meaning of construction work

(1)  Construction work means any of the following work—

(a)  the construction, alteration, repair, restoration, maintenance, extension, demolition or dismantling of buildings or structures, whether permanent or not, forming, or to form, part of land;

(b)  the construction, alteration, repair, restoration, maintenance, extension, demolition or dismantling of any works forming, or to form, part of land, including walls, roadworks, powerlines, telecommunication apparatus, aircraft runways, docks and harbours, railways, inland waterways, pipelines, reservoirs, water mains, wells, sewers, industrial plant and installations for land drainage or coast protection;

(c)  the installation in any building, structure or works of fittings forming, or to form, part of land, including heating, lighting, air-conditioning, ventilation, power supply, drainage, sanitation, water supply, fire protection, security and communications systems;

(d)  the external or internal cleaning of buildings, structures and works, so far as it is carried out in the course of their construction, alteration, repair, restoration, maintenance or extension;

(e)  any operation that forms an integral part of, or is preparatory to or is for completing, work of the kind referred to in paragraph (a), (b) or (c), including—

(i)  site clearance, earthmoving, excavation, tunnelling and boring; and

(ii)  the laying of foundations; and

(iii)  the erection, maintenance or dismantling of scaffolding; and

(iv)  the prefabrication of components to form part of any building, structure or works, whether carried out on-site or off-site; and

(v)  site restoration, landscaping and the provision of roadways and other access works;

(f)  the painting or decorating of the internal or external surfaces of any building, structure or works;

(g)  carrying out the testing of soils and road making materials during the construction and maintenance of roads;

(h)  any other work of a kind prescribed by regulation.

(2)  To remove doubt, it is declared that construction work includes building work within the meaning of the Queensland Building and Construction Commission Act 1991.

(3)  However, construction work does not include any of the following work—

(a)  the drilling for, or extraction of, oil or natural gas;

(b)  the extraction, whether by underground or surface working, of minerals, including tunnelling or boring, or constructing underground works, for that purpose.

Section 65 is the gateway provision. If your scope sits in one of the limbs (a)–(h), the BIF Act’s progress-payment machinery is engaged. A single contract often contains both construction work and other scope; only the construction-work component attracts the Act, so you need to identify it carefully (see also the discussion of “construction contract” in s 64).

The courts read s 65 in its natural sense — not stretched in either direction to capture or exclude marginal cases. In Capricorn Quarries Pty Ltd v Inline Communication Construction Pty Ltd [2012] QSC 388; [2013] 2 Qd R 1, Jackson J rejected an argument that BCIPA (the predecessor regime) should be given a “remedial” construction:

Having regard to the context as discussed, in my view, a “natural” construction of the relevant definitions of BCIPA in this case is to be preferred to an approach which seeks to extend the operation of BCIPA by a “liberal interpretation” to be engaged in with the purpose of increasing the width of the class of persons who are entitled to the benefit of a payment claim and correspondingly increasing the width of the class of persons who are subject to BCIPA’s restriction and obligations.

The language chosen by Parliament to define “construction contract”, “construction work” and “related goods and services” has no purpose other than to draw the line between who is in and who is out of those classes.  There seems to be little logic in seeking to stretch that language either way.  In saying this, I take a “natural” construction to be that arrived at by the usual process of the application of the common law of statutory interpretation, as affected by statute, but without a presumptive approach.

Two further early-authority points are worth noting. In Walter Construction Group Ltd v CPL (Surry Hills) Pty Ltd [2003] NSWSC 266, Nicholas J rejected the argument that an extension-of-time claim did not relate to construction work — EOT claims are within the regime. In Patrick Stevedores Operations (No 2) Pty Ltd v McConnell Dowell Constructors (Aust) Pty Ltd [2014] NSWSC 1413, Ball J held that the costs of plant and materials the claimant ordered just to put itself in a position to comply with its contract, and the costs of demobilising equipment from site, were neither construction work under the contract nor related goods or services.

“Forming, or to form, part of land”

The phrase “works to form part of land” in s 65(1)(b) is, as Fryberg J put it in Theiss Pty Ltd v Warren Brothers Earthmoving Pty Ltd [2011] QSC 345 at [28], “evidently a phrase of wide meaning”. His Honour rejected the suggestion that the works must be additions to the land, and said the section “should be given a practical interpretation which will minimise potential areas of dispute” (at [32]).

On mining leases and the meaning of “land”, the Court of Appeal in J & D Rigging Pty Ltd v Agripower Australia Ltd & Ors [2013] QCA 406 confirmed that the word takes its ordinary meaning. Applegarth J (Holmes JA and Boddice J agreeing) said:

BCIPA reallocates financial risk between the parties to a construction contract to which it applies. Section 10 should be interpreted so that its ordinary words are able to be applied by parties to determine whether or not a contract is subject to BCIPA. If possible, the statute should be interpreted so that it is capable of being applied in a practical way by parties to a construction contract or a proposed construction contract. The ordinary meaning of the words in s 10 anticipates a practical inquiry into the physical relationship between an item and land, by asking in the case of s 10(1)(a) whether the building or structure forms, or is to form, part of land. In the case of the demolition or dismantling of a building or structure, the inquiry is whether the building or structure forms part of land. The inquiry is into the physical state of things, not the intention of parties at the time the building or structure was constructed, possibly many years earlier.

…

The word “land” in s 10 has the extended meaning contained in the Acts Interpretation Act as well as its ordinary meaning. The expression “forming, or to form part of land” uses the word “form” in its ordinary sense.

The words of s 10 do not call for an inquiry into whether the plant formed part of land according to common law rules about the ownership of fixtures. They do not import a requirement that the plant be owned by the owner of the land upon which it was constructed as a result of this having been the objective intention of certain parties. Requirements of the law of real property about ownership of things affixed to land are not imported into s 10. Instead, the degree of annexation will be relevant to the issue of whether or not a thing forms part of land.

Section 65(1)(b) – permanence not required

In Theiss Pty Ltd v Warren Brothers Earthmoving Pty Ltd [2011] QSC 345, Fryberg J rejected an argument that works under paragraph (b) must be intended to be permanent (at [31]).

The Victorian decision in BCS Infrastructure Support Pty Ltd v Jones Lang Lasalle (NSW) Pty Ltd [2020] VSC 739 illustrates the breadth of the “works” limb. Stynes J held that the maintenance of baggage conveyor systems, aerobridges, check-in kiosks and baggage return carousels at an airport fell within construction work under s 5(1)(a) of the NSW SOP Act (the equivalent of s 65(1)(b) of the BIF Act), because those structures were permanently attached to the terminal building. The contract was therefore a construction contract for SOP-Act purposes.

Section 65(1)(e) – integral, preparatory and completing operations

Paragraph (e) only catches operations that have a sufficient nexus with construction work in paragraphs (a), (b) or (c). In Wiggins Island Coal Export Terminal Pty Ltd v Monadelphous Engineering Pty Ltd & Ors [2015] QSC 307, Philip McMurdo J explained the connection point this way:

the close connection between an operation within paragraph (e) and construction work of a kind within paragraphs (a), (b) or (c).  An operation falls within paragraph (e) only because of the part it plays in construction work which, according to the terms of paragraphs (a), (b) and (c), involves a connection with certain land.

Section 65(1)(e)(i) — site clearance, earthmoving, excavation, tunnelling and boring. In Theiss Pty Ltd v Warren Brothers Earthmoving Pty Ltd [2011] QSC 345, Fryberg J held that clearing and grubbing work fell within this paragraph — see also Theiss Pty Ltd v Warren Brothers Earthmoving Pty Ltd [2012] QCA 276; [2013] 2 Qd R 75.

The mining boundary was tested in Cadia Holdings Pty Ltd v Downer EDI Mining Pty Ltd [2020] NSWSC 1588. Stevenson J held that preparatory work which is not for the actual purpose of extracting minerals falls within construction work and is not knocked out by the “mining exception” in s 5(2) of the NSW SOP Act (the equivalent of s 65(1)(e) of BIFSOPA). Cadia had engaged Downer to perform tunnelling, boring and underground works to construct access to the proposed undercut and extraction levels of a mine. Cadia argued the scope was expressly excluded under the “Mining Exception” in s 5(2)(b) of the SOP Act. The Court read the exception narrowly: the tunnelling, boring or constructing of underground works must be for the actual purpose of extracting minerals. Where the object and purpose of the contract is not extraction itself, the exception does not apply.

Section 65(1)(e)(iv) — prefabrication of components. In Wiggins Island Coal Export Terminal Pty Ltd v Monadelphous Engineering Pty Ltd & Ors [2015] QSC 307, Philip McMurdo J held that a prefabricated shiploader and “tripper” to be installed on a wharf at a coal export terminal were properly characterised as components to form part of that wharf and within s 10(1)(e) of the predecessor Act.

Section 65(3) – the mining and oil/gas exclusion

Coal is a “mineral”. In the context of the predecessor s 10(3) of BCIPA, coal has been treated as a mineral for the purposes of what is now s 65(3): Theiss Pty Ltd v Warren Brothers Earthmoving Pty Ltd [2011] QSC 345 at [39] (Fryberg J).

“Extraction” is read narrowly. Fryberg J in Theiss [2011] QSC 345 at [42] read the exclusion tightly:

I accept that the work performed by Warren was a necessary part of opening the coal mines. But that is not the issue. The exemption given by s 10(3)(b) is not expressed to apply to work done for the purpose of opening or as preparatory to operating a mine. The words used are much more limited than that. They focus purely on the process of extraction. In my judgment the ordinary meaning of the word, considered in isolation, does not apply to the work done by Warren.

Douglas J adopted the same approach in HM Mire Pty Ltd v National Plant and Equipment Pty Ltd [2012] QSC 4 at [13]:

I agree with his Honour’s reasons dealing with the proper construction of the section. The facts of this case are also similar to the facts then being considered by his Honour. It also seems to me to be necessary to construe s 10(3) in the context set by s 10(1) which describes a relatively broad set of circumstances amounting to construction work. It would detract unnecessarily from the apparent purpose of the legislation and the normal understanding of s 10(1) and its hierarchy in the section to extend the meaning of “extraction of minerals” to cover work associated with such extraction where the legislature, as Mr Ambrose submitted, could readily have made such a purpose clear by the use of familiar language of wider meaning than this phrase. There is no reason why s 10(3) should be read so as to displace or render nugatory the meaning of s 10(1).

The narrow approach was approved by the Court of Appeal in Theiss Pty Ltd v Warren Brothers Earthmoving Pty Ltd [2012] QCA 276; [2013] 2 Qd R 75, 91 [62]–[63] (Philippides J, Holmes and White JJA agreeing). The practical message: work that merely enables a mine to operate, as opposed to the actual extraction of minerals, will usually still be construction work.

Section 66 – Meaning of related goods and services

Section 66 BIF Act – Meaning of related goods and services
66 Meaning of related goods and services

(1)  Related goods and services, in relation to construction work, means any of the following—

(a)  goods of the following kind—

(i)  materials and components to form part of any building, structure or work arising from construction work;

(ii)  plant or materials (whether supplied by sale, hire or otherwise) for use in connection with the carrying out of construction work;

(b)  services of the following kind—

(i)  the provision of labour to carry out construction work;

(ii)  architectural, design, surveying or quantity surveying services relating to construction work;

(iii)  building, engineering, interior or exterior decoration or landscape advisory services relating to construction work;

(iv)  soil testing services relating to construction work;

(c)  goods and services, relating to construction work, of a kind prescribed by regulation.

(2)  In this chapter, a reference to related goods and services includes a reference to related goods or services.

Section 66 picks up supplies that are not themselves construction work but feed into it — materials, plant hire, labour-only crews, design and engineering consultancies, surveyors, soil testers and the like. If a supply does not fall within s 65, this is the second gateway to test before assuming the BIF Act does not apply.

A “natural” construction

The same interpretive starting point applies. In Capricorn Quarries Pty Ltd v Inline Communication Construction Pty Ltd [2012] QSC 388; [2013] 2 Qd R 1, Jackson J held that a “natural” construction should be given to “construction contract”, “construction work” and “related goods and services”, rather than a remedial or liberal one:

Having regard to the context as discussed, in my view, a “natural” construction of the relevant definitions of BCIPA in this case is to be preferred to an approach which seeks to extend the operation of BCIPA by a “liberal interpretation” to be engaged in with the purpose of increasing the width of the class of persons who are entitled to the benefit of a payment claim and correspondingly increasing the width of the class of persons who are subject to BCIPA’s restriction and obligations.

The language chosen by Parliament to define “construction contract”, “construction work” and “related goods and services” has no purpose other than to draw the line between who is in and who is out of those classes.  There seems to be little logic in seeking to stretch that language either way.  In saying this, I take a “natural” construction to be that arrived at by the usual process of the application of the common law of statutory interpretation, as affected by statute, but without a presumptive approach.

“Supply”

In Patrick Stevedores Operations (No 2) Pty Ltd v McConnell Dowell Constructors (Aust) Pty Ltd [2014] NSWSC 1413, Ball J held that plant and materials the claimant ordered, and the cost of removing equipment from site, were not the supply of related goods or services. The plant and materials had been ordered to put the claimant “in a position where it could comply with its contractual obligations” — that internal preparation was not, by itself, a supply of related goods or services to the other party.

Section 66(1)(a) – “for use in connection with”

The connector phrase “for use in connection with” was construed in Theiss Pty Ltd v Warren Brothers Earthmoving Pty Ltd [2011] QSC 345. Fryberg J held:

In my judgment, “for use in connection with” is not satisfied simply by proving that the plant or materials supplied were used in connection with the carrying out of construction work.  Warren’s submission to that effect should be rejected. “For” is a word of wide denotation.  In the present context it carries a purposive meaning.  It suggests that the phrase must be satisfied at the outset of the transaction, before the plant or materials are used.  Evidence of the use to which plant or materials were put may support an inference as to the purpose for which they were at that time to be used, but such evidence could not satisfy the phrase without such an inference.

That conclusion is supported by the manner in which “related goods and services” is used in the Act. The most important use is in the definition of construction contract, a definition which relevantly refers to a “contract, agreement or other arrangement”. As noted above, in most other contexts in which the expression is used it is used to refer to related goods and services supplied under a construction contract. That suggests that the contract or arrangement will supply an important part of the evidence by which the proposed use of the plant or materials is to be identified. The use must be able to be identified at the time of the contract or arrangement or possibly, if the supply takes place at an earlier time, at the time of the supply.

That construction was adopted by Jackson J in Capricorn Quarries Pty Ltd v Inline Communication Construction Pty Ltd [2012] QSC 388; [2013] 2 Qd R 1, 11 [49]. The practical takeaway: to bring a plant-or-materials supply within s 66(1)(a)(ii), the construction-related purpose must be identifiable at the time of contract (or, where supply is earlier, at the time of supply) — not back-justified by how the goods were eventually used.

“Services”

In Sheppard Homes Pty Ltd v FADL Industrial Pty Ltd [2010] QSC 228, Fryberg J held that the supply of a licence for drawings does not fall within the definition of services. The agreement in question was between a consultant and a builder, under which the consultant licensed the builder to use certain plans for building work. His Honour held that the agreement was not the provision of related services under the Act.

Architectural services have been held to fall within “related goods and services”: Peter’s of Kensington v Seeksucker Pty Ltd [2008] NSWSC 897.

Project management services have likewise been held to fall within “related goods and services”: Biseja Pty Ltd v NSI Group Pty Ltd [2006] NSWSC 835.

Practice tips

  • Run the s 65 test scope-by-scope. A single contract often mixes construction work with other supplies; only the construction-work portion (and related goods and services for it) attracts the BIF Act.
  • If the work is not in s 65, do not stop — check s 66. Plant hire, labour hire, design, surveying, soil testing and similar inputs are caught even though they are not construction work themselves.
  • For s 65(1)(b), do not assume temporary or non-permanent works are out. Theiss and BCS Infrastructure confirm permanence is not required, and ongoing maintenance of structures attached to a building can qualify.
  • For mining and resources scope, identify whether your work is “for the actual purpose of extracting minerals” or merely enables the mine to operate. Following Cadia and Theiss, preparatory and access works are usually still construction work despite s 65(3).
  • For supply-only contracts, document the construction purpose at the time of contract (or supply, if earlier). Per Theiss, “for use in connection with” is judged at the outset, so a clear scope and project reference on the order makes the s 66(1)(a)(ii) test much easier.
  • Be cautious with IP-only or licence-style consultancy deals. Following Sheppard Homes, a bare licence to use plans is not a related service — if you want BIF coverage, structure the engagement as the provision of architectural, design or project management services connected to the construction work.

Chapter 3 · Sections 67 & 70

Reference date

The reference date is the engine that drives the BIF Act’s payment regime. Section 67 tells you when a reference date arises — either by the contract or by a statutory default — and section 70 tells you that, from each reference date, a contractor or supplier is entitled to a progress payment. If there is no available reference date, there is no valid payment claim, and any adjudication that follows is at risk of being set aside.

Section 67 – Meaning of reference date

Section 67 BIF Act – Meaning of reference date
67 Meaning of reference date

(1)  A reference date, for a construction contract, means—

(a)  a date stated in, or worked out under, the contract as the date on which a claim for a progress payment may be made for construction work carried out, or related goods and services supplied, under the contract; or

(b)  if the contract does not provide for the matter—

(i)  the last day of the month in which the construction work was first carried out, or the related goods and services were first supplied, under the contract; and

(ii)  the last day of each later month.

(2)  However, if a construction contract is terminated and the contract does not provide for, or purports to prevent, a reference date surviving beyond termination, the final reference date for the contract is the date the contract is terminated.

Section 67 broadly mirrors the previous definition under Schedule 2 of BCIPA, with two textual tweaks: it drops the words “undertaken to be carried out” and reduces “named month” to “month”. The earlier case law on the BCIPA wording remains a reliable guide.

The real change is sub-s (2). It creates a statutory final reference date on the day of termination where the contract is silent, or where the contract tries to stop reference dates surviving termination. The explanatory notes describe this as closing a loophole exposed by the High Court’s decision in Southern Han Breakfast Point Pty Ltd (in liq) v Lewence Construction Pty Ltd:

Clause 67 is a new provision defining the term ‘reference date’ for the purposes of this chapter. The definition of reference date also provides for when a construction contract is terminated and does not provide for a reference date surviving beyond termination. In such cases, the final reference date for when payment claim may be made is the day the contract is terminated.

“Stated in, or worked out under, the contract”

Paragraph (a) of the definition is the contract limb. The reference date is whatever date the contract states, or whatever date can be calculated by applying facts to a formula in the contract. In State of Queensland v T & M Buckley Pty Ltd [2012] QSC 265 at [17]–[19], Margaret Wilson J explained the difference between the two:

By paragraph (a) of the statutory definition, “reference date” means a date “stated in” or “worked out under” the contract. Whether a date is one “stated in” the contract depends on the proper construction of the words used in the contract without reference to extrinsic evidence (with the possible exception of evidence of the matrix of facts).

According to the Oxford English Dictionary Online, one of the meanings of “work out” is —

“To go through a process of calculation or consideration so as to arrive at the solution of (a problem or question), to solve; also, to reckon out, calculate.”

Just as a mathematical problem may be solved or “worked out” by applying values to a given formula, so may a “reference date” be “worked out under the contract” by applying facts to a formula found within the contract. In my view counsel for the first respondent’s submission ignores the true import of the expression “worked out under”. If it were correct, there would arguably be no distinction between a date “stated in” the contract and one “worked out under” it, because in each case the date would be one ascertained by construction of the contract without reference to extrinsic evidence.

That “worked out” idea was taken further in Lean Field Developments Pty Ltd v E & I Global Solutions (Aust) Pty Ltd & Anor [2014] QSC 293, where Applegarth J (at [30]) accepted that a reference date can be calculated by reference to facts or post-contract conduct — including conduct that is within the power of one party to bring about:

[30] The term “worked out” is not defined in the Act. The ordinary meaning of “worked out” in this context connotes a process of calculation. Absent some contextual basis to not apply that ordinary meaning, the statutory definition of “reference date” would seem to allow a reference date to be worked out by applying a formula to facts that are capable of being ascertained. For example, it might provide that a claim for a progress payment may be made 14 days after event X occurs. There is no compelling reason why the fact in question should not be something within the power of a party to bring about. For instance, a contract might provide that a claim for a progress payment may be made 60 days after the contractor commences the works and every 60 days thereafter. The first and subsequent reference dates are worked out by reference to the conduct of the contractor after the formation of the contract in commencing work. In such a case the existence of a reference date is conditioned by the conduct of one of the parties. There is nothing absurd about such an outcome, and it might be inconvenient if the parties could not agree such a provision. It would be odd if the parties could not negotiate, subject to limits imposed by s 99, a formula to work out a reference date which applied to the conduct of one of the parties, rather than rely on the default position set by sub-paragraph (b) of the statutory definition. Subject to limits imposed by s 99 and the assumption in s 12 that there will be a reference date under a construction contract, the parties should be free to agree provisions for the timing of claims for progress payments by reference to post-formation events, including events that depend on a party’s conduct.

Applegarth J in Lean Field (at [31]) went further again, holding that a reference date can even be worked out by reference to an event which is not certain to occur (such as a milestone or the submission of a particular document).
