Here, although the adjudication decision should have been made pursuant to an earlier notice, Mr Williams’ failure to give such a notice seems to have been caused by the imprecision of Gisley Investments’ own email in failing to identify itself as a payment schedule explicitly on its face. The consequence of the delay was that Gisley Investments had a further opportunity to provide a payment schedule and a later notification of the adjudication application that it decided to ignore. In either case, the real objects of the Act of providing notice of the claim and an opportunity to respond by provision of a schedule and by appearing before the adjudicator have been met and there is no good reason why the adjudicator’s decision should be treated as a nullity.

On the facts, the claimant had to apply by 3 December 2009 but did not lodge until 1 February 2010 because it had not treated a relatively informal email as a payment schedule. Daubney J upheld the validity of the adjudication decision.

That outcome is fact-specific. The earlier authorities show how seriously the courts treat the timing rule. In Nebmas Pty Ltd v Sub Divide Pty Ltd & Ors [2009] QSC 92, McMurdo J said:

As that judgment makes clear, the conclusion in Kell & Rigby, that the requirement for a notice under s 21(2) is mandatory, does not answer the present question, which is whether the adjudication is void where the adjudicator has decided, albeit wrongly, that there had been compliance with that provision. The judgment of Hodgson JA in Brodyn indicates that this requirement for a notice under s 21(2) is not an essential requirement, in the sense that it was an essential precondition of the existence of an adjudicator’s determination. With regard to the purpose of this legislation, there is no reason why this requirement within s 21(2) should be an essential requirement although, for example, the time limit within s 21(3) should not be essential: cf Project Blue Sky Inc v Australian Broadcasting Authority. Of course s 21(2) provides that an adjudication application “can not be made” unless there is such a notice and these precise words are not replicated in s 21(3). Nevertheless, s 21(3) provides that an adjudication application “must be made” within the times there set out. In each case the words are “emphatic”, and in my view there is no basis for distinguishing between the two provisions on the basis of this difference in words.

Accordingly, the fact, as I have found, that there was non-compliance with s 21(2) does not have the result for which the present applicant contends. The declaratory relief which it seeks must be refused. It also follows that the interlocutory injunction restraining the first respondent from filing the adjudicator’s certificate should be discharged.

Nebmas was followed by Fryberg J in De Neefe Signs Pty Ltd v Build1 (Qld) Pty Ltd; Traffic Technologies Traffic Hire Pty Ltd v Build1 (Qld) Pty Ltd [2010] QSC 279. On the effect of non-compliance, his Honour treated compliance with s21(2) as a precondition to the adjudicator’s jurisdiction:

That question must be answered by construing the opening words of the subsection. I acknowledge that they must be construed in the light of the two conditions, particularly the nature and content of those conditions. I acknowledge the force of the view that those conditions seem on their face purely procedural matters aimed at ensuring the provision of natural justice and perhaps reducing the chance that the case will actually go to adjudication. There is however no reason why Parliament should not if it so chooses make fulfilment of conditions of that nature essential to the making of an application. In my judgment, the words “an adjudication application … cannot be made” produce that result. They should be given their natural meaning. If by statute an application cannot be made, then anything purporting to be one cannot be an application. The existence of an application is a basic and essential requirement of an adjudicator’s determination.

That is the same conclusion as was reached in Kell & Rigby Pty Ltd v Guardian International Properties Pty Ltd.

The effect of the contrary conclusion is the re-wording of s 21(2) to read “unless the adjudicator thinks” in place of “unless” (and it consequentially requires a change of tense in the two conditions). While the subject matter of BCIPA can hardly be compared with the liberty of the subject, there is powerful (albeit dissenting) authority against adopting that technique of statutory interpretation.

In Kuatro Build Pty Ltd v Elite Formwork Group Pty Ltd [2025] NSWSC 372, Hmelnitsky J held that Elite’s adjudication application was validly made under s17 of the SOP Act (the equivalent of section 79 BIFSOPA). Elite served its payment claim on 28 November 2024 and lodged its adjudication application on 3 January 2025, which fell within the statutory timeframe. The Court did not identify any compliance issue, and no jurisdictional objection was raised on lodgement or timing.

The approved form – section 79(2)(a)

The application must be in the ‘approved form’. In Iris Broadbeach Business Pty Ltd v Descon Group Australia Pty Ltd & Anor [2023] QSC 290, Williams J held that the form the claimant gave the respondent was not the ‘approved form’. The claimant had given the respondent the QBCC PDF Form, which is automatically generated when the Electronic Form is submitted on the QBCC website.

Williams J explained that the ‘approved form’ is the form approved for the purpose of the claimant applying to the registrar for adjudication — the actual application document the claimant lodges with the QBCC, electronically or in hardcopy. The QBCC PDF Form is a receipt produced after lodgement and is not the approved form.

Two important practical updates: the QBCC Registry has since changed how it processes adjudication applications, and on 6 June 2024 the Queensland Parliament passed the Residential Tenancies and Rooming Accommodation and Other Legislation Amendment Act 2024, which made significant amendments to the BIF Act. The Explanatory Note to the Third Reading explicitly says those amendments respond to Iris Broadbeach. Those June 2024 amendments have rendered the ‘approved form’ principle from Iris Broadbeach nugatory.

Identifying the payment claim and payment schedule – section 79(2)(c)

The application must identify the payment claim and any payment schedule it relates to. In Kangaroo Point Developments MP Property Pty Ltd v RHG Construction Fitout and Maintenance Pty Ltd [2021] QSC 30, Dalton J held an adjudication application failed s79(2)(c) because the claimant identified a different document instead of the payment schedule the respondent had actually issued. Her Honour treated the word “must” in s79(2)(a) and s79(2)(c) as denoting a mandatory requirement, in contrast to the word “may” in s79(2)(e), which gives the applicant a choice about whether to put on submissions.

By contrast, in National Management Group Pty Ltd v Biriel Industries Pty Ltd trading as Master Steel & Ors [2019] QSC 219, Wilson J held that an adjudication application satisfied s79(2)(c) despite a number of errors, because “the adjudication application document refers to the project name, the reference date and the amount due.”

In Parkview Constructions Pty Limited v Total Lifestyle Windows Pty Ltd t/a Total Concept Group [2017] NSWSC 194, Hammerschlag J considered service of an adjudication application under s17 of the NSW Act (the equivalent of s21 of the Act). His Honour held it is plain that what is served on the respondent must itself be in writing, and service via a USB stick did not equate to service of writing stored on it within the meaning of the Act.

Submissions – section 79(2)(e)

Submissions are optional but, in practice, almost always included. The meaning of ‘submission’ was considered by McDougall J in Austruc Constructions Ltd v ACA Developments Pty Ltd; ACA Developments Pty Ltd v Sarlos [2004] NSWSC 131. The plaintiff argued there was a distinction between a ‘submission’ and ‘evidence’, with the former being the theory or reasoning propounded. McDougall J rejected that distinction:

I do not think that the word “submissions”, in either s 17(3) or s 22(2), should be limited as Mr Corsaro submitted. Firstly, I do not think that the ordinary English meaning of the word “submission” is limited in the way that ACA contends. It is certainly correct to say that one of the definitions given by the Shorter Oxford English Dictionary is “[t]he theory of a case put forward by an advocate”. However, the same dictionary also defines the word to mean “the act of submitting a matter to a person for decision or consideration”; and it gives other definitions as well. Further, the Macquarie Dictionary defines “submission” as including “the act of submitting ... the condition of having submitted … submissive conduct or attitude … that which is submitted … law an agreement to abide by a decision or obey an authority in some matter referred to arbitration … “.

It is apparent from the definitions given by both dictionaries that the “ordinary English meaning” for which ACA contends is a specific application of the more general meaning, to the effect of “that which is submitted for decision or consideration”.

Secondly, I think that the better view of s 17(3) is that it does not limit the matters that may be put to an adjudicator in an adjudication application. In this context, I think that the contrast between the mandatory language of paragraphs (a) to (g), and the discretionary language of paragraph (h), is clear.

Thirdly, and in any event, I think that it is s 22(2) that governs the situation. It will be recalled that that subsection specifies the only matters that an adjudicator may take into account. Those matters include, through paragraph (c), the relevant payment claim “together with all submissions (including relevant documentation) …”. Not only do the parenthesised words show that the legislature had in mind that the word ”submissions” was not to be construed narrowly, as ACA contends; they show specifically that the submissions may include relevant documentation in support.

It follows, I think, that if a claimant chooses to include, as part of the relevant documentation supporting its payment claim, a statutory declaration whereby relevant matters are, in effect, verified, then that statutory declaration will form part of the material to be considered by the adjudicator. Equally, if a claimant includes such a statutory declaration in its adjudication application, that is part of the “submission” to be considered.

This approach was followed in Queensland in Syntech Resources Pty Ltd v Peter Campbell Earthmoving (Aust) Pty Ltd [2011] QSC 293, [32] (Daubney J). Including submissions is directory rather than mandatory: Abacus Funds Management Ltd v Davenport [2003] NSWSC 1027, [19] (McDougall J).

‘Relevant to the application’ — the limit on submissions

Submissions can only go so far. In John Holland v Cardno MBK (NSW) Pty Ltd [2004] NSWSC 258, Einstein J considered the NSW equivalent of s21(3) and the introduction of new reasons in an adjudication application:

The adjudication application will relate to a particular payment claim and payment schedule [section 17 (3) (f)]. The central significance of the entitlement of the applicant to include submissions as part of its adjudication application is because those submissions have to be supportive of the payment claim. Those submissions cannot constitute a payment claim or part of it. The central significance of the entitlement of the respondent to include submissions as part of its adjudication response is because those submissions have to be supportive of the payment schedule. Those submissions cannot constitute a payment schedule or part of it.

New reasons (and new material) in an adjudication application

Section 82(4) of the Act stops a respondent from putting forward, in its adjudication response, reasons that were not in its payment schedule. There is no equivalent provision aimed at claimants — but the courts have arrived at a similar restriction by a different route. The leading cases are John Holland v Cardno MBK (NSW) Pty Ltd [2004] NSWSC 258 and Minister for Commerce v Contrax Plumbing (NSW) Pty Ltd [2004] NSWSC 823.

In John Holland, Einstein J said in the context of section 20(2B) of the NSW Act:

The primary touchstone it seems to me, is section 20 (2B). Whilst a claimant which provides the most minimal amount of information in its payment claim may even so, be seen to technically comply with section 13, such a claimant will expose itself to an abortive adjudication determination if it be that:

the respondent is simply unable to discern from the content of the payment claim, sufficient detail of that claim to be in a position to meaningfully verify or reject the claim: hence not then being in a position to do otherwise than to reject the whole of the claim on the basis of its inability to verify any part of the claim;

the claimant then elects to include the missing detail in the adjudication application with the inexorable consequence that the respondent is barred by section 20 (2B) from dealing with that detail/matter in its adjudication response;

the adjudicator relies in determining the adjudication application upon the detail supportive of the payment claim which first emerged as part of the adjudication application.

For those reasons whilst it is not permissible to construe section 13 as providing that in order to be a valid payment claim, such a claim must do more than satisfy the requirements stipulated for by subsection 2 (a), (b) and (c), the consequence to a claimant which does not include sufficient detail of that claim to be in a position to permit the respondent to meaningfully verify or reject the claim, may indeed be to abort any determination.

His Honour then turned to documents supplied for the first time in the adjudication application:

The deploying for the first time in the adjudication application, of supporting documentation will require careful attention and becomes a matter of degree and detail. However in the main I do not see that a respondent which, by reason of insufficient information supplied with the payment claim, is unable to verify that claim, and says as much in the payment schedule [only later to receive as part of the adjudication application, the supporting documentation which should have been earlier supplied in order to permit a meaningful payment schedule response], will be otherwise than barred by section 20 (2B) from including in its adjudication response reasons for withholding payment arising by reference to the later supporting documentation. It could not be said that those reasons were already included in the payment schedule provided to the claimant. A complaint about inability to verify a claim because of insufficient information is not synonymous with reasons for dealing with a properly supported claim.

Einstein J’s reasoning was revisited by McDougall J in Minister for Commerce v Contrax Plumbing (NSW) Pty Ltd [2004] NSWSC 823:

In John Holland, it was submitted to Einstein J that considerations of procedural fairness demanded that a restriction similar to that contained in s 20(2B) be read into s 17(3), to the effect that a claimant in an adjudication application is restricted to raising only matters canvassed in its payment claim. While his Honour thought that considerations of “logic”, and “consistency” with the situation of respondents, suggested that this submission be accepted (at [4]), his conclusion was that the “accepted principles of statutory construction” would not permit the suggested implication to be made (at [21]). This conclusion was bolstered by the fact that, in contrast to the situation of respondents when preparing payment schedules pursuant to s 14(3), it is not an “essential condition” of s 13 that the claimant include any reasons whatsoever in a payment claim (at [18]).

Einstein J had reached the result by a different route — he held that where an adjudication application puts a claim on a basis not advanced in the payment claim, the adjudicator has no jurisdiction to deal with it, and there would also be a denial of natural justice (at [41]). That was because s20(2B) would prevent the respondent from including in its adjudication response any reasons relating to the new claim, and the respondent could not deal with a new claim except by doing what s20(2B) prohibited. Determining a new claim on a basis the respondent could not answer was, his Honour said, a denial of natural justice.

McDougall J read John Holland this way:

What Einstein J said in John Holland was that a claimant that did not provide sufficient details in its payment claim to enable the respondent to verify or reject (ie, assess) the claim could not include the missing details in its adjudication application. That was because, since the respondent was barred by s 20(2B) from replying to those details (ie, of responding in its adjudication response in a way that did deal with the merits of the claim) the result “may indeed be to abort any determination”: at [23]. His Honour said, alternatively, that an adjudicator did not have power to consider materials supplied by a claimant in its adjudication application which went outside the materials provided in the payment: at [24]. Materials would go outside what had already been provided if they fell outside the ambit or scope of that earlier material.

McDougall J refused, however, to accept that an adjudication application could not address matters raised in the payment schedule:

It would be quite extraordinary if the statutory regime, on its proper construction, prevented an applicant for adjudication from dealing with issues raised by the respondent to adjudication in its payment schedule. Such a construction would mean, in effect, that the applicant would be required to anticipate in its payment claim, and deal with at length, every possible argument that the respondent might rely upon. That would have the effect of increasing enormously the complexity and expense of the statutory procedure: something quite at odds with the statutory objects set out in s 3 and reinforced in the Second Reading Speech. It would also mean that, notwithstanding the best attempts of the applicant to foresee and answer all possible arguments, it might be defeated if the ingenuity of the respondent or its lawyers turned up yet further arguments.

I do not believe that the legislature intended such consequences to flow from the scheme that it enacted. Nor do I think that there is anything in what Einstein J said in John Holland that requires me to conclude, notwithstanding the views that I have expressed, that the legislature did intend such bizarre consequences to follow.

See also the commentary under section 88. In Probuild Constructions (Aust) Pty Ltd v Shade Systems Pty Ltd [2016] NSWSC 770, Emmett AJA held there was no denial of procedural fairness where an adjudicator made a determination for an amount greater than the amount claimed in the adjudication application. The amount claimed in the application was less than the amount in the payment claim because the applicant had conceded a number of matters. On a fair reading of the payment claim, payment schedule, adjudication application and adjudication response, his Honour found there was no denial of procedural fairness:

In all of the circumstances, I do not consider that, on a fair reading of the Payment Claim, the Payment Schedule, the Adjudication Application, and the Adjudication Response, Probuild was denied procedural fairness.

The fee

Under s79(2)(d) the application must be accompanied by the fee prescribed by regulation. No fee, no valid application.

The role of the Registrar

Under s79(5), the Registrar must, within 4 business days after the application is received, refer it to a person eligible to be an adjudicator under section 80. The Registrar’s job at this stage is administrative referral, not gatekeeping the merits.

Giving the application to the respondent – section 79(3)

Section 79(4) requires the claimant to give the respondent a copy of the application, and any submissions, within 4 business days after lodging. The case law on the equivalent NSW ‘serve as soon as possible’ rule is directly relevant.

In Niclin Constructions Pty Ltd v SHA Premier Constructions Pty Ltd & Anor [2019] QSC 91, Ryan J held that for an adjudication application to be validly served on the respondent in accordance with s21(5) BCIPA (equivalent to s79(3) of the Act):

  • a copy of the QBCC form 6 (now form S79) must be served on the respondent as part of the adjudication application; and
  • the adjudication application, including the QBCC form 6 (now form S79), must be served on the respondent as soon as possible.

Her Honour reasoned that given the “brutally fast timeframes” imposed by BCIPA, service of the form was significant because it told the adjudicator when to determine the case in the knowledge that all material was before them — including any adjudication response, which would follow the application and be due within 10 business days under BCIPA. That decision was upheld on appeal: in Niclin Constructions Pty Ltd v SHA Premier Constructions Pty Ltd & Anor [2019] QCA 177, the Queensland Court of Appeal upheld the primary judge’s finding that s21(5) BCIPA (equivalent to s79 of the Qld Act) requires service of an adjudication application on the respondent “as soon as possible” after lodgement with the registrar.

In McCarthy v TKM Builders Pty Ltd & Anor [2020] QSC 301, Martin J held an adjudication application was not “given to the respondent” in accordance with s79(3) because service of the supporting submissions was invalid. The submissions could only be obtained by opening a Dropbox link sent in an email to the respondent. Applying McMurdo J’s reasoning in Basetec, the Court held the respondent did not become aware of the contents merely by being referred to a Dropbox link. It was not enough that the respondent’s solicitors saw the submissions when the email was forwarded; the Court required a positive action on the part of the receiver.

In Equinox Construction Pty Ltd v Henning & Anor [2021] QSC 223, Ryan J declared an adjudication determination void because the claimant failed to “give” a copy of the adjudication application to the respondent under s79(3). The claimant said it had posted the application in accordance with sections 39 and 29A of the Acts Interpretation Act 1954 (Qld). The respondent said it never received it. Ryan J held the claimant must be in a position to strictly prove service so disputes can be quickly and efficiently resolved by adjudication. The Court found that:

  • evidence of having used a “postage paid” envelope is not sufficient under section 39 or 39A because the claimant did not explain how postage paid envelopes were to be used when paying for postage of items contained in them and failed to give evidence about how the postage was calculated/paid for; and
  • evidence of attending a post office on a specific date is not sufficient to establish how the posting was effected.

The position is more forgiving where the differences between what was lodged and what was served are trivial. In Equa Building Services Pty Ltd v KLG Trading Pty Ltd [2021] NSWSC 1674, Stevenson J held the claimant had sufficiently served a copy of the adjudication application on the respondent even though it was not an exact reproduction, because the differences were trivial and inconsequential. The claimant had served the application electronically via an online “lockbox” system and provided a hard copy to the respondent. The respondent argued the hard copy differed because: the respondent could not access a video on a USB; three documents were missing; some photographs were illegible; and documents were mislabelled. Stevenson J held that s17(5) of the NSW SOP Act (equivalent to s79(3)) is capable of degrees of non-compliance if it does not prejudice the substantial effect of the objectives of the Act. The differences here were trivial: the USB content was accessible (the claimant’s own software could not open it); the missing documents were a payment claim template and an ASIC search of the developer; the photographs were clearly copies of the documents uploaded electronically; and the mislabelling did not matter because the documents themselves were in both versions.

In Iris Broadbeach Business Pty Ltd v Descon Group Australia Pty Ltd & Anor [2023] QSC 290, Williams J held an adjudicator did not have jurisdiction because the claimant had not provided ‘a complete copy’ of the adjudication application to the respondent. The claimant had given the respondent the ‘QBCC PDF Form’, automatically generated by the QBCC when the ‘approved form’ is submitted on the QBCC website, and Williams J found this was not the approved form. Her Honour found the differences between the forms were ‘not trivial’ and were ‘such as to result in the copy of the QBCC PDF form not being a “copy” of the Electronic Form’. It was possible to give a full copy of the form electronically completed, and there was no evidence to suggest that doing so was ‘onerous or time-consuming such as to make it inconsistent with the timeframes in the statutory regime’. The Court noted this could be done by taking a screen shot of each page of the QBCC’s platform when electronically making an adjudication application.

Note: the amendments to the BIF Act in June 2024 have rendered the ‘approved form’ principle from Iris Broadbeach nugatory.

Practice tips

  • Pick the right window before you lodge. Map the facts to s79(2)(b)(i), (ii) or (iii) and diary the deadline in business days from the correct trigger event — the due date, the last day a payment schedule could have been given under s76, or the day you received the schedule.
  • If you are using the s78(2)(b) / s76 second-chance route, document the dates of the original payment claim, the s76 notice, and the date the respondent could last have given a schedule. Limb (B) of the 30-day window depends on this.
  • Lodge using the ‘approved form’ that the QBCC requires for lodgement. Do not assume the receipt PDF you get back is what must be served on the respondent — serve a complete copy of what you actually lodged.
  • Make sure the application identifies the payment claim and the payment schedule it relates to (project name, reference date, amount due at a minimum). Identifying the wrong document, as in Kangaroo Point Developments, can be fatal.
  • Get the application and any submissions into the respondent’s hands within the 4 business day window in s79(4). Do not rely on Dropbox links, USB sticks alone, or untracked “postage paid” envelopes — McCarthy, Parkview and Equinox show how easily service goes wrong. Keep proof of receipt.
  • Use submissions to support the payment claim and to answer issues raised in the payment schedule — do not use them to introduce a new claim, new reasons or new supporting documents that should have been with the original payment claim. The adjudicator may decline to consider them, or the determination may be at risk.

Chapter 3 · Sections 80 & 81

Appointment of adjudicator

Once the Registrar refers your adjudication application to an adjudicator, two short provisions govern who can take the job and how they take it. Section 80 sets the eligibility floor — an adjudicator who is a party to the contract, or who has a prescribed conflict, cannot adjudicate. Section 81 sets the mechanics — the adjudicator has 4 business days to accept or reject, and acceptance happens by serving a written notice on the claimant, the respondent and the Registrar.

Eligibility (s80)

Section 80 BIF Act – When adjudicator ineligible to adjudicate
80 When adjudicator ineligible to adjudicate

An adjudicator is not eligible to adjudicate an adjudication application if the adjudicator—

(a)  is a party to the construction contract to which the application relates; or

(b)  has a conflict of interest as prescribed by regulation.

Acceptance and notice (s81)

Section 81 BIF Act – Appointment of adjudicator
81 Appointment of adjudicator

(1)  If the registrar refers an adjudication application to an adjudicator for a decision, the adjudicator must, unless the adjudicator has a reasonable excuse, accept or reject the referral within 4 business days after the referral is made.

(2)  The adjudicator may accept the referral by serving written notice of the acceptance on the claimant, the respondent and the registrar.

(3)  The adjudicator may reject the referral by notifying the registrar of the refusal.

(4)  If the adjudicator rejects the referral or does not accept it within the time required under subsection (1)—

(a)  the registrar must refer the adjudication application to another adjudicator within 4 business days after becoming aware of the refusal or failure; and

(b)  no fee is payable for referring the adjudication application to another adjudicator.

(5)  The other adjudicator may accept the referral by serving written notice of the acceptance on the claimant, the respondent and the registrar.

(6)  The other adjudicator may reject the referral by notifying the registrar of the refusal.

(7)  On accepting a referral of an adjudication application under subsection (1) or (5), an adjudicator is taken to have been appointed to decide the application.

The mechanism is simple. The Registrar refers; the adjudicator has 4 business days to accept or reject; acceptance is by written notice served on all three (claimant, respondent and Registrar); rejection is by notifying the Registrar. If the adjudicator rejects or simply does not respond in time, the Registrar must re-refer to another adjudicator within 4 business days, and no further fee is payable. Appointment is taken to occur on acceptance — that is the moment the adjudicator’s clock starts.

Acceptance through an agent

An adjudicator does not have to sign and serve the notice of acceptance personally. An agent can do it. In Peekhrst Pty Ltd v Wallace & Anor [2007] QSC 159, Douglas J considered the equivalent of section 81 (then s23) and held:

There is nothing in the section to suggest that an adjudicator may not authorise an agent to accept on his behalf. Given the speed with which these claims are to be dealt with, it may well be the case from time to time that a nominated adjudicator may not be available immediately to sign and serve a notice of acceptance personally. That does not mean that those steps may not be taken by an agent.

…

Even if the acceptance of the application through an agent constituted a breach of the Act, which I do not accept, there is nothing to suggest that the use of an agent in circumstances like this should lead to invalidity; cf. Project Blue Sky v Australian Broadcasting Authority (1998) 194 CLR 355, 390-391 at [93].

The practical takeaway is that a notice of acceptance signed and served by the ANA (or another agent) on the adjudicator’s behalf is good enough. Don’t waste time challenging an appointment on that basis alone.

Service of notice of acceptance

Once the adjudicator (or their agent) serves the notice of acceptance, the Act’s service rules do the rest — including deeming receipt where the notice has been properly posted. In Randhawa v Serrato [2009] NSWSC 170, the adjudicator’s notice of acceptance was served by post. The plaintiff led evidence that it had not received the notice and used that to attack the adjudicator’s determination. The Court held that ‘if delivery is not disproved the fact of non-receipt does not displace the result that the delivery is deemed to be effected at the time at which it would have taken place in the ordinary course of the post’ (citing Fancourt v Mercantile Credits (1983) 154 CLR 87 at 97). Evidence of non-receipt, on its own, was not enough to disprove delivery, and the challenge to the determination failed.

Practice tips

  • When the notice of acceptance arrives, diary it — that is the moment the adjudicator is appointed under s81(7) and the decision-making clock starts running.
  • Don’t object to the notice of acceptance just because it was signed by an ANA staff member or other agent rather than the adjudicator personally. Peekhrst says that is fine.
  • If you genuinely have not received the notice of acceptance within the expected window, contact the Registrar promptly and ask for confirmation of when and how it was served — under Randhawa, mere non-receipt will not undo a properly posted notice.
  • Keep an eye on conflicts. If you know the appointed adjudicator has a connection to a party or to the contract, raise it with the adjudicator and the Registrar straight away under s80, before the adjudicator gets too far into the determination.
  • If the first adjudicator rejects (or doesn’t accept in time), expect a re-referral to another adjudicator within 4 business days at no extra fee — you do not need to lodge again or pay another fee.

Chapter 3 · Sections 82 & 83

Adjudication response

Once the adjudicator notifies the respondent that the application has been accepted, a short window opens for the respondent to file an adjudication response. Sections 82 and 83 of the BIF Act control what can go in that document and how long the respondent has to lodge it. Both rules are unforgiving: the response is locked to the reasons already given in the payment schedule, and the clock is measured in business days from receipt of the application papers.

Section 82 BIF Act – Adjudication response
82 Adjudication Response

(1)  After being given notice of an adjudicator’s acceptance of an adjudication application under section 81, the respondent may give the adjudicator a response to the adjudication application (the adjudication response).

(2)  However, the respondent must not give an adjudication response if the respondent failed to give the claimant a payment schedule as required under section 76.

(3)  The adjudication response—

(a)  must be in writing; and

(b)  must identify the adjudication application to which it relates; and

(c)  may include the submissions relevant to the response the respondent chooses to include.

(4)  However, the adjudication response must not include any reasons (new reasons) for withholding payment that were not included in the payment schedule when given to the claimant.

(5)  The adjudicator may require the respondent to resubmit the adjudication response without the new reasons.

Form and content (s82)

The structural rules are simple: the response must be in writing, must identify the application it answers, and may include whatever submissions the respondent chooses — subject always to s82(4). A respondent that did not serve a payment schedule under s76 is shut out of the response process entirely.

Although the response cannot expand the reasons given in the schedule, it is not limited to a word-for-word repeat of the schedule. In TQM v Dasein [2004] NSWSC 1216 at [30], McDougall J (commenting on the equivalent NSW provision) explained that the response can amplify reasons already given, with more detail or supporting evidence, even though it cannot raise new ones:

It was submitted for Dasein that Mr Davenport had in any event considered the payment schedule provided by TQM, so that (because of s 20(2B) of the Act) he had in substance considered its case. I do not accept that submission. It is correct to that, by s 20(2B), a respondent cannot include in its adjudication response any reason for withholding payment that has not been included in its payment schedule. However, it would be open to a respondent to argue, either in greater detail or with perhaps more supporting evidence, reasons that had been advanced. If it were intended that an adjudication response should do no more than mirror, word for word, a payment schedule, then there is little point to the ability under s 20(1) for a respondent to lodge an adjudication response. In that context, it may be noted that an adjudication response by s 20(2)(c) “may contain such submissions relevant to the response as the respondent chooses to include”. That must be qualified by subs (2B) so that the submission is not only relevant to the response but also in amplification of reasons earlier given; but it is a clear indication that the adjudication response may do more than parrot the terms of the payment schedule.

Submissions and supporting documents

“Submissions” in s82(3)(c) is read broadly. In Syntech Resources Pty Ltd v Peter Campbell Earthmoving (Aust) Pty Ltd & Ors [2011] QSC 293, Daubney J observed:

The word “submissions” in s 26(2)(d) is not to be construed narrowly; indeed, the words of the section show specifically that the submissions may include relevant documentation in support.

That principle drove the result in Trysams Pty Ltd v Club Constructions (NSW) Pty Ltd [2007] NSWSC 941, where an expert report appeared in the adjudication response but had not been attached to the payment schedule. Hammerschlag J held that the report was either part of a submission or relevant documentation in support of a submission “duly made” by the plaintiff (at [49]). The report was ‘foreshadowed’ in the schedule and ‘[t]he report did not represent any shift from that position. It was provided in support of it’ (at [56]). His Honour continued:

Despite the interim nature of an adjudication, natural justice nevertheless clearly required the adjudicator to consider the report unless (even if erroneously) he determined that it was, or was part of, a submission not duly made. In this case, he made no such determination. This amounted to a substantial failure to afford natural justice which worked practical injustice on the plaintiff and rendered the whole adjudication void.

Section 22(2)(d) required the adjudicator, in the circumstances, to consider the report because it was part of submissions duly made. In so far as a failure to do so was jurisdictional error in the sense that a legislative requirement essential to the existence of a determination was not met, he made such an error.

The line between “explanatory” material and a new reason can be very fine. In Wiggins Island Coal Export Terminal Pty Ltd v Monadelphous Engineering Pty Ltd & Ors [2015] QSC 307, the respondent attached an EOT expert report to its response that had not been served with the payment schedule. Philip McMurdo J was unable to find that the expert report did not raise a reason for withholding payment that was not in the schedule (at [65]). In Syntech Daubney J reached the opposite view on the documents in front of him, treating the supporting material as “explanatory” reasons only (at [25]). And in Owners Strata Plan 61172 v Stratabuild Ltd [2011] NSWSC 1000, Macready AJ held that the schedule was “appropriately worded and specific enough to ‘sufficiently indicate’ the reasons for withholding payment”:

However, even if the adjudicator was incorrect, had he correctly considered section 20(2B) of the Act at paragraphs 31 to 35 of his reasons? I think the defendant is correct in submitting that one must focus on the substance rather than the form of the adjudicator’s statements. The adjudicator expressed his understanding of ss 14(3) and 20(2B) of the Act. The adjudicator’s view was that, consistent with the policies of fairness and promptness, the full reports should be included with the payment schedule to allow a claimant to properly articulate an adjudication application and prevent a fresh case being brought about in reply after that application was made. The defendant says that the adjudicator did not misconstrue the Act in a way that led to a misconception of his functions.

Unfortunately, the adjudicator did not appreciate how the courts have interpreted the use of the word “indicate” in s 14(3) and the difference between “reasons” and submissions” when used in the legislation.

A practical example: in Syntech, the schedule stated that there should be a $142,134.25 deduction for wet hire and that a review of past payments showed the claimant had been overpaid by $127,618.40. The response then attached supporting spreadsheets. The adjudicator refused to look at the spreadsheets on natural-justice grounds (treating them as material that should have been served with the schedule). The Court held that the failure to consider the spreadsheets was a breach of an essential requirement and amounted to jurisdictional error.

The “no new reasons” rule (s82(4))

Section 82(4) is the heart of the regime: the response cannot smuggle in fresh reasons for withholding payment. In John Holland Pty Ltd v Walz Marine Services Pty Ltd & Ors [2011] QSC 39, Margaret Wilson J (considering s24 of BCIPA, the equivalent of s88 of the Act) put it this way:

Thus, the issues to be decided by the adjudicator are defined in the payment claim and the payment schedule. The respondent must state its reasons for wholly or partially withholding payment in the payment schedule. While the parties may make submissions in support of their respective positions in the adjudication application and the adjudication response respectively, the respondent cannot use the adjudication response as a vehicle to advance reasons for withholding payment which it failed to include in its payment schedule.

The rule has been applied repeatedly. In Thiess Pty Ltd & John Holland Pty Ltd v Civil Works Australia Pty Ltd [2010] QSC 187; [2011] 2 Qd R 276, Daubney J held that an adjudicator did not commit jurisdictional error by refusing to consider clauses raised in the response but not in the schedule. In GW Enterprises Pty Ltd v Xentex Industries Pty Ltd [2006] QSC 399, Lyons J approved the adjudicator’s decision ‘he could not take into account any matters raised by the current applicant which they had not raised in the payment schedule’ (at [36]) and held:

it is imperative that any reasons for withholding payment must be raised in the payment schedule or they cannot be raised at all.

The policy reason was put squarely by Sackar J in State Water Corporation v Civil Team Engineering Pty Ltd [2013] NSWSC 1879 at [63]:

… Once a payment claim and payment schedule have been exchanged, the claimant’s decision of whether or not to take the further step of lodging an adjudication application will depend on its assessment of the respondent’s reasons for non-payment as disclosed in its payment schedule. There are obvious and powerful policy considerations in ensuring that the respondent discloses all of its reasons for non-payment in its payment schedule. The difficulties a claimant would experience if faced with an adjudication response which raised reasons for non-payment which were not included in the payment schedule, are amplified by the fact that the Act does not grant to the claimant a right to reply to the respondent’s adjudication response.

In Brambles Australia Ltd v Davenport [2004] NSWSC 120, Einstein J held that the adjudicator was right to disregard a response that relied on a contract clause (cl 24.1) that had never been mentioned in the schedule:

In my own view, the proper approach, bearing in mind the time constraints built into the scheme, is to be reasonably strict where, as here for example, enforcing the injunction to be found in section 20(2B). Precision and particularity was required in the payment schedule to a degree reasonably sufficient to apprise the parties of the real issues in the dispute. If clause 24.1 was to have been relied upon it had to be expressly identified. Insofar as the adjudication response here went outside the reasons for withholding payment which were included in the payment schedule, it was appropriate for the adjudicator to disregard the adjudication response.

In BuiltCom Construction Pty Ltd v VSD Investments Pty Ltd atf the VSD Investments Trust; VSD Investments Pty Ltd atf The VSD Investments Trust v Builtcom Construction Pty Ltd [2025] NSWSC 250, Peden J upheld the adjudicator’s refusal to consider VSD’s additional site inspection documents that were not referenced in its payment schedule. Under s 20(2B) (equivalent to section 82(4) BIFSOPA) respondents are prohibited from introducing new reason for withholding payment that were not included in the payment schedule. The Court followed EnerMech Pty Ltd v Acciona Infrastructure Projects Australia Pty Ltd (2024) 115 NSWLR 56, where Basten AJA confirmed that claims for damages or restitution are generally outside an adjudicator’s jurisdiction. Peden J agreed that VSD’s defect setoffs were not particularised and amounted to global claims for damages, which the adjudicator had no power to assess.

Quantum reasons vs “other” reasons

An attempt is sometimes made to draw a line between reasons that explain “why the scheduled amount is less” and the broader category of “reasons for withholding payment”. In John Holland Pty Ltd v Roads & Traffic Authority of New South Wales & Ors [2007] NSWCA 19, Hodgson JA rejected that distinction:

In my opinion, this distinction does not justify a narrow view as to what amounts to reasons for withholding payment. If a respondent does not propose to pay any amount included in the payment claim for any reasons said to justify non-payment of that amount, then in my opinion that is withholding payment and the reasons are reasons for withholding payment. It does not matter whether the reasons relate to non-performance of work, bad work, set-offs or cross-claims of any kind, contractual provisions limiting the claimant’s right to payment or statutory provisions limiting the claimant’s right to payment, or indeed any other suggested justification. Any other view would do violence to the language “withholding payment for any reason”, and be contrary to the plain purpose of s.20(2B) to avoid new submissions being introduced late in a process going ahead on a brief and strict timetable. I agree with what Palmer J said on this matter in Mulitiplex Constructions Pty. Limited v. Luikens [2003] NSWSC 1140 at [65]-[68].

Philip McMurdo J adopted this passage in Wiggins Island Coal Export Terminal Pty Ltd v Monadelphous Engineering Pty Ltd & Ors [2015] QSC 307, [52]:

The evident intent of s 24 is to prevent the unfairness to a claimant which could follow from a respondent being allowed to contest its alleged liability for a reason which it had not advanced ahead of the adjudication application. If WICET’s argument was accepted, that beneficial operation of s 24(4) would be confined to one kind of reason for non-payment which would have no logical distinction from another.

In Baguley Build Pty Ltd v Olcon Concrete & Construction Pty Ltd [2025] QSC 126, Copley J held that the adjudicator did not fall into jurisdictional error by concluding that the applicant’s adjudication response included “new reasons” for withholding payment that had not been stated in the payment schedule, thereby not contravening s 82(4) of the BIF Act. The adjudicator found that the response attempted to rely on reasons derived from earlier payment schedules without specifying them in relation to the new consolidated claim, and thus determined that they could not be considered. His Honour affirmed that it is a matter “for the adjudicator to determine” whether reasons are new or properly included, and relied on Civmec Electrical & Instrumental Pty Ltd v Southern Cross Electrical Engineering Ltd [2019] QSC 300, where Mullins J drew a distinction between failing to consider properly made submissions (which may amount to jurisdictional error) and mischaracterising submissions (which is an error within jurisdiction). This approach was also supported by Applegarth J in Niclin Constructions Pty Ltd v Robotic Steel Fab Pty Ltd (2023) 16 QR 336.

Reasons going to the adjudicator’s jurisdiction

The s82(4) bar does not extend to arguments about the adjudicator’s jurisdiction. Those arguments can be raised in the response even if they were not in the schedule, because they could not have been raised in the schedule. McDougall J explained the point in Rail Corporation (NSW) v Nebax Constructions [2012] NSWSC 6:

More significantly, it appears to me, s 20(2B) talks of “reasons for withholding payment”. That is not what is at issue here. The question that was raised for the adjudicator’s consideration was whether he had any jurisdiction to hear the multiple applications that were lodged.

In Olympia Group (NSW) Pty Ltd v Hansen Yuncken Pty Ltd [2011] NSWSC 165, Ball J said at [11], of a similar argument put to him for consideration, that s 20(2B) prevented the respondent:

“from raising in its adjudication response a reason for not making a payment that was not raised in its payment schedule. It did not prevent it from raising grounds on which it was asserted that the adjudicator did not have jurisdiction to make a determination”.

I agree. The point could not have been taken in the payment schedule. Thus, the alternative submission referred to at [8] above and the submission referred to at [10] above must fail.

In Watkins Contracting Pty Ltd v Hyatt Ground Engineering Pty Ltd [2018] QSC 65, Brown J held the respondent had not raised ‘new reasons’ where:

  • the payment schedule stated that Watkins proposed to pay ‘nil’, because there was no reference date to support the payment claim, as the contract had been terminated or frustrated; and
  • the adjudication response included detailed submissions as to the grounds of termination, including that the contract contained implied terms.

Her Honour held the response merely elaborated matters already raised, and added in obiter that even if the reasons had been new, because they went to jurisdiction the adjudicator was bound to consider them and s24(4) BCIPA would not apply.

In Bega Valley Shire Council v Kenpass Pty Ltd [2024] NSWSC 399, Nixon J refused to set aside an adjudication determination, including on the ground that the adjudicator had wrongly characterised reasons in the response as “new” under s20(2B). At [49] his Honour held that “any error by the Adjudicator in interpreting the Payment Schedule, or in understanding the reasons given in it, is not a reviewable error”. Whether new reasons have been raised is a matter for the adjudicator.

The other side of that coin is that, where the adjudicator does consider a new reason, the determination is exposed. In Acciona Agua Australia Pty Ltd v Monadelphous Engineering Pty Ltd [2020] QSC 133, Bond J declared part of a determination void where the adjudicator had accepted two grounds raised in the contractor’s response — that the subcontractor should be prevented from taking advantage of its breach, and that an alleged breach of an implied term entitled the contractor to set off — that had no counterpart in the payment schedule. Considering those new reasons offended s82(4) and breached s88(3)(b); the affected part of the determination was void with the balance left binding.

In Total Lifestyle Windows Pty Ltd v Aniko Constructions Pty Ltd & Anor [2021] QSC 92, Martin J declared part of a determination void where the adjudicator had applied a contract clause that the respondent first relied on in the response. The court held that:

  • section 69(c) of the BIF Act requires a respondent to include all of its reasons for withholding payment in its payment schedule and not just those that are specifically raised or prompted by the payment claim;
  • section 82(3)(c) of the BIF Act must be read subject to section 82(4) which provides that an adjudication response must not include any reasons for withholding payment that were not included in the payment schedule when given to the claimant; and
  • once an adjudicator considers a matter that it is not permitted to pursuant to section 82 of the BIF Act, then section 88(3)(b) has been breached, whether or not the consideration leads to a particular decision.

The same dispute returned in Total Lifestyle Windows Pty Ltd v Aniko Constructions Pty Ltd (No. 2) [2021] QSC 231. There, the claimant had suspended works after non-payment; without telling the claimant, the respondent engaged a replacement contractor. In its response (but not in the schedule), the respondent argued it had only engaged the new contractor after the suspension period and so had not removed any work for s98(3) purposes. The adjudicator accepted that argument. Freeburn J held the adjudicator had considered a new defence not included in the schedule, in breach of s88(3), and declared the affected parts of the revised determination void.

The reverse situation is also a trap. In Castle Constructions Pty Ltd v Napoli Excavations and Civil Pty Ltd [2023] NSWSC 348, the NSW Supreme Court quashed a determination because the adjudicator failed to engage with a submission in the response. Both parties accepted the contract was terminated; the dispute was about the date. The adjudicator proceeded as if the works had been suspended rather than terminated and so never grappled with the respondent’s termination submission. Because the submission had been duly made, that failure was material and amounted to jurisdictional error.

Section 24(5) BCIPA – complex claims

Section 24(5) provided that where an adjudication application is about a complex payment claim, a respondent may include reasons for withholding payment not included in the payment schedule. This subsection was unique to Queensland under BCIPA, introduced in December 2014 by the Amendment Act. Cases from other jurisdictions, and Queensland cases under BCIPA before 15 December 2014 and after the commencement of the BIF Act, should be read with that history in mind.

Section 83 BIF Act – Time for making adjudication response
83 Time for making adjudication response

(1)  If responding to a standard payment claim, the respondent must give the adjudicator the adjudication response within the later of the following periods to end—

(a)  10 business days after receiving the documents mentioned in section 79(4);

(b)  7 business days after receiving notice of the adjudicator’s acceptance of the adjudication application.

(2)  If responding to a complex payment claim, the respondent must give the adjudicator the adjudication response within the later of the following to end—

(a)  15 business days after receiving the documents mentioned in section 79(4);

(b)  12 business days after receiving notice of the adjudicator’s acceptance of the adjudication application.

(3)  However, if responding to a complex payment claim, the respondent may apply to the adjudicator for an extension of time, of up to 15 additional business days, to give the adjudication response to the adjudicator.

(4)  The application must—

(a)  be in writing; and

(b)  be made within the later of the following periods to end—

(i)  5 business days after receiving the documents mentioned in section 79(4);

(ii)  2 business days after receiving notice of the adjudicator’s acceptance of the adjudication application; and

(c)  include the reasons for requiring the extension of time.

(5)  If the application is granted, the respondent may give the adjudicator the adjudication response no later than the end of the extension of time granted by the adjudicator.

(6)  If the respondent gives the adjudicator an adjudication response under this section, the respondent must give a copy of the response to the claimant not more than 2 business days after giving the response to the adjudicator.

Timing (s83): standard vs complex deadlines

For a standard payment claim, the respondent must lodge the response by the later of 10 business days after receiving the s79(4) application papers and 7 business days after receiving notice of the adjudicator’s acceptance. For a complex claim, those windows extend to 15 business days from the application papers and 12 business days from the notice of acceptance.

For complex claims only, the respondent can apply to the adjudicator for up to 15 extra business days. The extension request itself has to be in writing, has to set out the reasons, and must be made within the later of 5 business days from the application papers or 2 business days from the notice of acceptance. Miss those windows and the extension is unavailable. Once the response is filed, the respondent has 2 business days to serve a copy on the claimant.

“Receiving” the application

Each of the s83 windows runs from the date the respondent “received” either the application or the notice of acceptance. The Act draws a deliberate distinction between “service” and “receipt”: the further commentary on s102 deals with what counts as receipt.

The point matters because miscalculating receipt can cost the respondent its entire response. In CMF Projects Pty Ltd v Masic Pty Ltd & Ors [2014] QSC 209, an adjudicator’s notice of acceptance was sent not to the respondent’s registered office but to a post office box. Daubney J held the adjudicator had erred by treating delivery to the post office box as receipt of the notice. In Rise Constructions v El-Hajj [2019] VSC 818, Digby J refused to set aside a determination for jurisdictional error or want of procedural fairness where the adjudicator had decided the notice of acceptance was served on a particular date and, on that basis, declined to consider an out-of-time response (the response was treated as filed outside the s21(1)(b) Vic Act window equivalent to s83(1)(b) of the BIF Act).

Practice tips

  • Treat your s69 payment schedule as the script for the adjudication response. If a reason is not in the schedule, s82(4) will lock you out of running it before the adjudicator — and on the authorities (Acciona Agua, Total Lifestyle Windows) any determination that does consider the new reason is exposed to being set aside.
  • Use the response to amplify, evidence and develop reasons already in the schedule (TQM v Dasein, Trysams) — but don’t mistake ‘more detail’ for ‘new ground’. If the supporting documents (expert reports, spreadsheets, calculations) exist, attach them to the schedule rather than holding them back for the response.
  • Diary the s83 deadline as soon as the s79(4) papers and notice of acceptance arrive. Calculate both the ‘papers’ clock and the ‘notice of acceptance’ clock and use the later date — do not assume the longer one applies without checking.
  • If the claim is complex and you need more time, lodge the s83(3) extension request inside the very tight 5/2 business day window. Miss it and you cannot get more than the standard 15/12 days; the request must be in writing and must give reasons.
  • Watch the ‘receipt’ trap. The clock runs from receipt, not service (CMF Projects v Masic, Rise Constructions v El-Hajj). Confirm where the application and notice of acceptance were actually delivered and when they were actually received before counting business days.
  • Do not forget the 2 business day copy-to-claimant obligation in s83(6); and remember that if you failed to serve a payment schedule under s76, s82(2) means you cannot file a response at all.

Chapter 3 · Sections 84–86

Adjudication procedures

Once the adjudicator has the application and any response, sections 84 to 86 of the BIF Act govern how the adjudication actually runs — what powers the adjudicator can use to gather information, how quickly the decision must come out, and when the deadline can be pushed back. For a PM, these sections set the tempo of the dispute and define what you can and cannot expect from the process.

Section 84 BIF Act – Adjudication procedures
84 Adjudication Procedures

(1)  Subject to the time requirements under section 85, an adjudicator must decide the following as quickly as possible—

(a)  an adjudication application;

(b)  applications for extensions of time under section 83.

(2)  For a proceeding conducted to decide an adjudication application, an adjudicator—

(a)  must decide—

(i)  whether he or she has jurisdiction to adjudicate the application; and

(ii)  whether the application is frivolous or vexatious; and

(b)  may ask for further written submissions from either party and must give the other party an opportunity to comment on the submissions; and

(c)  may set deadlines for further submissions and comments by the parties; and

(d)  may call a conference of the parties; and

(e)  may carry out an inspection of any matter to which the claim relates.

(3)  If a conference is called, it must be conducted informally, and the parties are not entitled to any legal representation unless allowed by the adjudicator.

(4)  The adjudicator’s power to decide an adjudication application is not affected by the failure of either or both of the parties to make a submission or comment within time or to comply with the adjudicator’s call for a conference of the parties.

The adjudicator’s procedural powers (s84)

Section 84 gives the adjudicator a tight set of tools: decide jurisdiction and whether the application is frivolous or vexatious, request further written submissions, set deadlines, call a conference, and inspect the work. Conferences are informal and lawyers do not appear unless the adjudicator allows it. Crucially, if a party fails to respond to a request, comply with a deadline, or attend a conference, the adjudicator can still proceed and decide the application.

The role of the adjudicator

The starting point for what an adjudicator must actually do is that the adjudicator must “come to a view as to what is properly payable”: see Pacific General Securities Ltd & Anor v Soliman & Sons Pty Ltd & Ors [2006] NSWSC 13; Coordinated Construction Co Pty Ltd v J M Hargreaves (NSW) Pty Ltd [2005] NSWCA 228; (2005) 63 NSWLR 385; Thiess Pty Ltd v Warren Brothers Earthmoving Pty Ltd & Ors [2012] QSC 373.

In Pacific General Securities Ltd & Anor v Soliman & Sons Pty Ltd & Ors [2006] NSWSC 13, Brereton J described the role of the adjudicator in this way:

I therefore respectfully agree with the view tentatively expressed by Hodgson JA in Hargreaves: the adjudicator’s duty is to come to a view as to what is properly payable, on what the adjudicator considers to be the true construction of the contract and the Act and the true merits of the claim, and while the adjudicator may very readily find in favour of the claimant on the merits of the claim in the absence of a payment schedule or adjudication response, or if no relevant material is advanced by the respondent, the absence of such material does not entitle the adjudicator simply to award the amount of the claim without addressing its merits, which as a minimum will involve determining whether the construction work identified in the payment claim has been carried out, and what is its value.

Brereton J was picking up on the earlier judgment of Hodgson JA in Coordinated Construction Co Pty Ltd v J M Hargreaves (NSW) Pty Ltd [2005] NSWCA 228; (2005) 63 NSWLR 385:

The adjudicator’s duty is to come to a view as to what is properly payable, on what the adjudicator considers to be the true construction of the contract and the Act and the true merits of the claim.

The same idea was applied in Queensland by Ann Lyons J in Thiess Pty Ltd v Warren Brothers Earthmoving Pty Ltd & Ors [2012] QSC 373:

The adjudicator must also then make a genuine attempt to exercise his power in accordance with the BCIPA. An adjudicator is required to come to a determination based on the factors set out in s 26(2) of the BCIPA and is required to “turn their minds to, grapple with and form a view on all matters that they are required to ‘consider’.”

That settled position has more recently been qualified. In Ceerose Pty Ltd v A-Civil Aust Pty Ltd [2023] NSWCA 215, Payne JA (Ward ACJ and Basten AJA agreeing) declined to follow the “true merits” framing in Hargreaves and Pacific General Securities as a test for jurisdictional error where the respondent has not advanced material. Their Honours held at [75]:

The essence of [52] of Hargreaves is that the ‘adjudicator’s duty is to come to a view as to what is properly payable, on what the adjudicator considers to be the true construction of the contract and the Act and the true merits of the claim’ (emphasis added). There is a potential difficulty in this reasoning, at least in so far as it is being used as a test to identify jurisdictional error. The ‘true construction of the contract’ and the ‘true merits’ of the payment claim are, by reason of the structure of the Security of Payment Act I have set out above, matters where an error by an adjudicator will not be jurisdictional. As Basten JA explained at [66] of Hargreaves itself, in the light of the express restriction in s 20(2B) of the Act, there is merit in the conclusion that the adjudicator is not entitled to go beyond the terms of the adjudication response in rejecting part or all of the payment claim. Certainly, it is not a jurisdictional error for an adjudicator, having decided all the reasons advanced by the respondent were invalid, to then and without more, determine the amount of the progress payment in favour of the claimant based on the payment claim.”

That reasoning was applied in Bega Valley Shire Council v Kenpass Pty Ltd [2024] NSWSC 399, where Nixon J refused to set aside an adjudication determination, holding at [85]:

… Having decided that the Council had not advanced any valid objections to the quantification of delay damages in the Payment Schedule, or any objections in the Adjudication Response which he was permitted to consider, he proceeded “then and without more” to “determine the amount of the progress payment in favour of the claimant based on the payment claim”. It was not a jurisdictional error to do so.

His Honour at [86] also linked the result to the legislative purpose described in Probuild Constructions (Aust) Pty Ltd v Shade Systems Pty Ltd (2018) 264 CLR 1:

In Probuild at [47], the High Court identified the “clear legislative intention” of the statutory being regime as being “to ensure that the statutory entitlement can be determined and enforced with minimal delay”. It is consistent with this legislative intention that if a payment claim is made, and the adjudicator finds that the respondent does not advance in the payment schedule any valid reason for withholding payment, the adjudicator can “then and without more” determine the amount of the progress payment based on the payment claim, without independently examining and being satisfied as to the contractual basis for the claim, the merits of the claim, the proof of loss, or the quantification of damage. To the extent that there is an issue about any of those matters, the Security of Payment Act “defers the final determination of contractual rights to a different forum, in which the consequences of any erroneous determination can and must be taken into account”: Probuild at [47].

The contrasting (and earlier) line is illustrated by Laing O’Rourke Australia Construction Pty Ltd v Monford Group Pty Ltd [2018] NSWSC 491, where Stevenson J declared an adjudication void for jurisdictional error because the adjudicator awarded amounts to the claimant simply on the basis that the respondent had not disputed them in its response, without engaging with the merits. His Honour adopted Brereton J’s approach in Pacific General Securities Ltd v Soliman & Sons [2006] NSWSC 13: at a minimum the adjudicator must decide whether the construction work was carried out and what it is worth.

The Victorian summary by Vickery J in SSC Plenty Road Pty Ltd v Construction Engineering (Aust) Pty Ltd [2015] VSC 631 at [101] also remains a useful overview of the adjudicator’s task in valuing a claim.

Importantly for Queensland practice, the adjudicator’s consideration is not boxed in by the parties’ submissions. In BM Alliance Coal Operations Pty Ltd v BGC Contracting Pty Ltd & Ors [2012] QSC 346, Applegarth J said:

In carrying out the functions entrusted to him or her, an adjudicator must consider the submissions that have been made. That does not mean that the consideration of the merits of the payment claim is limited to issues actually raised by the submissions. An adjudicator’s task is to come to a view as to what is properly payable, but the submissions do not set the parameters of the application or its determination. It may be that there are no submissions or only partial submissions.

Where the adjudicator is “minded” to decide on a basis the parties have not raised, natural justice requires that the parties be given a chance to address it: BM Alliance Coal Operations Pty Ltd v BGC Contracting Pty Ltd & Ors [2012] QSC 346, [52] (Applegarth J).

Adjudication examples

  • SSC Plenty Road Pty Ltd v Construction Engineering (Aust) Pty Ltd [2015] VSC 631. The adjudicator dealt with 37 disputed variation claims. For 33 of them, Vickery J found that the adjudicator applied a “formula” that effectively put the onus on the respondent to justify withholding payment for each item, then adopted the payment claim figure once that onus was not met. The same approach was applied to three of five disputed deductions. Vickery J held that the adjudicator showed no real reasoning beyond adopting the claimant’s value, and fell into jurisdictional error in respect of all 33 variations and three deductions.

In Ingeteam Australia Pty Ltd v Susan River Solar Pty Ltd [2024] QSC 30, Applegarth J said at [86] that “a jurisidictional error will occur where a decision-maker finds a fact that is a precondition to the conferral of jurisdictional exists when it does not exist, or finds that such a fact does not exist when it does”. At [87] his Honour also confirmed that the principle summarised in Kirk v Industrial Court of New South Wales [2010] 239 CLR 531 applies to tribunals and other decision-makers:

An inferior court falls into a jurisdictional error ‘if it mistankenly asserts or denies the existence of jurisdiction.’

The adjudication determination in Ingeteam was declared void in part for jurisdictional error on three grounds:

  • Procedural fairness was denied because Ingeteam was not given an opportunity to make submissions on the jurisdictional point Susan River had raised in its adjudication response — that Ingeteam had breached section 42 of the QBCC Act when entering into the contract. Ingeteam was not given the chance to refute the alleged unlicensed building work, and was not given a chance to address the adjudicator’s conclusion that Ingeteam did not intend to comply with section 42.
  • The adjudicator was wrong to find that Ingeteam was carrying out unlicensed building work (floor repair). The work in question was the laying of a floating floor, and section 30 of the QBCC Regulation lists the laying of “carpets, floating floors or vinyl” as work that is not building work.
  • The adjudicator’s conclusion that Ingeteam intended not to comply with section 42 of the QBCC Act was not one any reasonable decision-maker could have reached on the evidence and submissions before him.

Susan River argued that, even on those findings, the errors were not material and so the determination should not be set aside. A jurisdictional error is material if the ultimate decision “could have been different” without the error. Applegarth J held all three errors were material because, had the adjudicator allowed submissions on the floor repair and on Ingeteam’s intention at the time of contract, there was a real possibility he would not have erred on either point and a real possibility procedural fairness would have led to a different conclusion on jurisdiction under both limbs of section 42.

Section 84(2)(a) – ‘must decide’ jurisdiction and frivolous/vexatious

Section 84(2)(a) requires the adjudicator to “decide whether he or she has jurisdiction to adjudicate the application”. That obligation was introduced by the Amendment Act and is a departure from the equivalent section 21(4) of the NSW Act.

The Act also requires the adjudicator to decide whether the application is frivolous or vexatious, but does not define those terms. Section 14A of the Acts Interpretation Act 1954 (Qld) directs that, in interpreting sections 84, 85 and 91, the construction that best achieves the purpose of the BIF Act — helping people in the building and construction industry get paid for the work they do — is to be preferred.

What “frivolous” or “vexatious” means in this context has not yet been judicially considered, and the explanatory notes do not explain the purpose of the amendment. The likely intention is to give the adjudicator a discretion to refuse a claimant any progress payment on that basis.

Section 84(2)(b), (c), (d) and (3) – discretionary tools

The equivalent NSW provisions were considered in Multiplex Constructions Pty Ltd v Luikens & Anor [2003] NSWSC 1140, where Palmer J said:

s.21(4) makes it clear that it is within the discretion of the adjudicator whether or not to take any of the steps set out in paragraphs (a) to (d). Exercise of that discretion one way or another will depend upon the adjudicator’s judgment as to whether or not he or she will be assisted in reaching a decision within the constraints, particularly the time constraints, imposed by the Act.

In Parliament Square Hobart Landowner Pty Ltd v Tonkin [2020] TASSC 30, Estcourt J held that an adjudicator’s jurisdiction extends to deciding whether contractual terms have been affected by waiver or estoppel, by reference to the powers under section 25(2) of the SOP Act (Tas) (equivalent to section 88(2) of the BIF Act). Parliament Square argued that the adjudicator could decide common law waiver but not equitable estoppel, because the latter would require an equitable remedy outside the contract. The court rejected that distinction and applied Multiplex Constructions Pty Ltd v Luikens [2003] NSWSC 1140: an adjudicator can consider acts that may found an estoppel in deciding whether a party can insist on strict compliance with the contract.

Section 84(3)(b) – the power to ask for further written submissions

The equivalent NSW power was considered in John Holland Pty Ltd v Cardno MBK (NSW) Pty Ltd [2004] NSWSC 258, where Einstein J said:

Whether or not any of these problems may be addressed by the adjudicator requesting further written submissions from either party may become the subject of curial examination on another occasion. However it would seem unlikely that the legislature would have intended the provisions of section 21 (4) (a) and (b) to permit a radical departure from the statutory scheme described above. Rather it seems likely that these sub-sections are to be read as permitting no more than additional submissions which clarify earlier submissions: those earlier submissions being constrained in the manner above described.

That passage was applied by Sackar J in State Water Corporation v Civil Team Engineering Pty Ltd [2013] NSWSC 1879. In that case the argument was that section 20(2B) of the NSW Act (equivalent to section 24(4) BCIPA) was not breached because the further submissions had been given in answer to the adjudicator’s specific request:

In my view, State Water's first submission should not be accepted. The provision permitting an adjudicator to request further submissions from the parties (s 21(4)(a)) cannot simply be exercised by an adjudicator to transform a party's submissions which were not duly made (by reason of s 20(2B)) to submissions duly made and capable of being considered by the adjudicator. That would empower adjudicators to defeat the underlying purpose of s 20(2B). There is authority supporting this view (John Holland Pty Ltd v Cardno MBK (NSW) Pty Limited & Ors [2004] NSWSC 258 at [26] per Einstein J).

In Minesco Pty Ltd v Anderson Sunvast Hong Kong Ltd [2017] VSC 299, Vickery J quashed an adjudication determination for denial of natural justice. The defendant had served documents that went beyond the adjudicator’s request for further information. The plaintiff wrote asking the adjudicator not to read or consider that material, but received no response and was left not knowing whether the additional information had been taken into account. His Honour held that the procedure adopted by the adjudicator was not such as to result in justice being seen to be done.

Failing to request further submissions

Adjudicators are not always required to chase further information. In Ku-Ring-Gai Council v Ichor Constructions Pty Ltd [2014] NSWSC 1534, Stevenson J considered that, given the “object of the Act is to provide a speedy resolution of progress claims”, the adjudicator could not be criticised “for failing to seek further information from the parties than that already placed before him”. That comment was made in the context of the adjudicator having concluded he could not decide the claimant’s delay damages claim on the material before him.

In Ausenco Operations Pty Ltd & Anor v Ferretti International Ottoway Pty Ltd & Anor [2020] SASC 46, Doyle J held that an adjudicator failed to afford the respondent procedural fairness by rejecting its claim for liquidated damages on a basis the claimant had not put forward in its adjudication application. The denial of procedural fairness was “plainly” substantial because the amount was material (over $200,000) and submissions the contractor was denied had a real prospect of influencing the result. The decision was infected by jurisdictional error and set aside. The court said that, if the adjudicator had intended to reject the liquidated damages on that basis, the adjudicator should have requested further submissions.

In 12 Bridge Street Epping Pty Ltd atf 12 Bridge Street Epping Unit Trust v D.R. Design (NSW) Pty Ltd [2022] NSWSC 866, Stevenson J refused to set aside an adjudication determination, holding that the adjudicator had power under section 21(4)(a) of the NSW SOP Act (the equivalent of section 84(2)(a) of the BIF Act) to request “further written submissions” even before an adjudication response was given, because the respondent’s email saying it had not received the adjudication application itself constituted a written “submission”. The respondent argued it had been denied natural justice because that submission was treated as its adjudication response, preventing it from filing one. His Honour rejected that contention: section 21(4)(a) does not stop an adjudicator from requesting further submissions and setting a deadline before an adjudication response is served, and there was no lack of procedural fairness because the submission was itself in answer to the adjudicator’s request and did not exclude the respondent from giving an adjudication response. Natural justice is dealt with in the commentary on section 88.

The adjudicator’s role in an uncontested adjudication

Palmer J in Brookhollow Pty Ltd v R&R Consultants Pty Ltd [2006] NSWSC 1 said that the adjudicator’s role and duties differ depending on whether the respondent participates:

In a contested adjudication, the adjudicator need consider only those provisions of the Act and of the contract which are relevant to the issues formulated by the parties in their submissions. In an adjudication in which the respondent does not participate the position of the adjudicator is, in my opinion, analogous to that of the Court when a plaintiff seeks the entry of judgment in default of an appearance by the defendant or where the defendant has failed to file a defence. In such a case, the Court still has a duty to decide the case according to truth and fact and if the plaintiff’s case appears on the face of the pleading or on the plaintiff’s evidence (or lack of it) to be fatally flawed, then the Court will refuse to enter judgment…

…
