(2)  Any adjudicator must, in any later adjudication application that involves the working out of the value of the construction work or of the related goods and services, give the work, or the goods and services, the same value as that previously decided by the adjudicator unless the claimant or respondent satisfies the adjudicator concerned that the value of the work, or the goods and services, has changed since the previous decision.

(3)  However, if a decision or order of a court changes the value of the construction work or of the related goods and services, the adjudicator must give the work, or the goods and services, the same value as changed by the decision or order unless the claimant or respondent satisfies the adjudicator concerned that the value of the work, or the goods and services, has changed since the decision or order.

Section 87 — the binding valuation rule

Section 87 binds a later adjudicator to a previously adjudicated value of construction work or related goods and services unless the parties show the value has changed. The provision rests on a critical distinction between valuation (or quantification) and entitlement. In Rothnere Pty Ltd v Quasar Constructions NSW Pty Ltd [2004] NSWSC 1151, McDougall J said an adjudicator’s decision may involve both, or one or the other. In John Goss Projects Pty Ltd v Leighton Contractors Pty Ltd [2006] NSWSC 789; (2006) 66 NSWLR 707, his Honour clarified:

Sections 9 and 10 make it clear that there is a distinction between the calculation of the amount of a progress payment (which is, ultimately, what the adjudicator is required to do) and the valuation of construction work. That is the distinction that I sought to point out (on reflection, in a way that was perhaps unduly brief and somewhat delphic) in para [43] of my decision in Rothnere.

The approach has been approved by the NSW Court of Appeal in Dualcorp Pty Ltd v Remo Constructions Pty Ltd [2009] NSWCA 69; (2009) 74 NSWLR 190 and by the Queensland Supreme Court in ACN 060 559 971 Pty Ltd v O’Brien [2007] QSC 91; [2008] 2 Qd R 396. The practical consequence is that amounts not referable to a particular item of construction work — such as liquidated damages — do not attract s87. O’Brien set the precedent: a subsequent adjudicator erred in relying on s27 BCIPA to adopt the previous adjudicator’s LD decision.

Where an adjudicator carries out a partial valuation of a large number of variation claims, the binding effect of s87 is limited to those claims actually valued: Hitachi Ltd v O’Donnell Griffin Pty Ltd [2008] QSC 135 (Skoien AJ — only six of 113 variation claims had been valued in the first adjudication).

The Queensland Court of Appeal in Bezzina Developers Pty Ltd v Deemah Stone (Qld) Pty Ltd & Ors [2008] QCA 213; [2008] 2 Qd R 495 held that a later adjudicator who is unaware of an earlier decision is not under an implied obligation to make inquiries:

does not know that there is, or that there probably is, a prior adjudication decision, no occasion arises for the exercise of the power to call for further submissions concerning any prior adjudication decision.

an obligation to seek further submissions from the parties on that topic should not be implied in s 27 when an express power to seek further submissions exists in s 25(4)(a). Implying such an obligation would also create practical difficulties in view of the very limited time available to adjudicators to make decisions and the absence of compulsive powers to obtain any prior adjudication.

In Iris Broadbeach Business Pty Ltd v Descon Group Australia Pty Ltd [2024] QSC 16, Wilson J found jurisdictional error where the adjudicator applied her own previous valuation of architectural work at $200,000 without engaging with Iris’s post-decision invoice suggesting only $183,780 was payable — a clear failure to consider whether the value had changed under s87(2). In Goyder Wind Farm 1 Pty Ltd v GE Renewable Energy Australia Pty Ltd & Ors [2024] SASC 108, Stein J declined to quash a determination on s87 grounds where successive payment claims for EOTs and delay costs were not properly characterised as a single claim for the same construction work.

In Harlech Enterprises Pty Ltd v Beno Excavations Pty Ltd [2022] ACTCA 42, the ACT Court of Appeal held that the equivalent of s87(2) limits the protection of an earlier valuation to the narrow situation the section describes — the Act’s purpose is not to apply issue estoppel to broader findings of adjudicators.

Section 89 BIF Act – Adjudicator may correct clerical mistakes etc.
89 Adjudicator may correct clerical mistakes etc.

(1)  This section applies if the adjudicator’s decision includes—

(a)  a clerical mistake; or

(b)  an error arising from an accidental slip or omission; or

(c)  a material miscalculation of figures or a material mistake in the description of a person, thing or matter mentioned in the decision; or

(d)  a defect of form.

(2)  The adjudicator may, on the adjudicator’s own initiative or on the application of the claimant or respondent, correct the decision.

(3)  The adjudicator may, if requested by the registrar, correct the decision.

Section 89 — the slip rule

Section 89 lets an adjudicator fix four discrete kinds of error: clerical mistake, accidental slip or omission, material miscalculation/material mistake in description, or defect of form. As McDougall J observed in Musico v Davenport [2003] NSWSC 977, the existence of the slip-rule power confirms that ordinary mistakes do not vitiate a determination — the legislature recognised that mistakes can be corrected at a final hearing, and the slip-rule power is permissive only.

In McNab Developments (Qld) Pty Ltd v MAK Construction Services Pty Ltd & Ors [2014] QCA 232, Jackson J explained that an accidental slip or omission in considering the schedule, submissions or documentation under s26(2) BCIPA does not invalidate the decision — precisely because s89 (and its predecessors) preserve the operation of the decision so it can be corrected. Whether the error is significant enough to warrant correction is a matter for the parties and the adjudicator.

The slip-rule does not allow the adjudicator to redo their reasoning. In Uniting Church in Australia Property Trust (Qld) v Davenport & Anor [2009] QSC 134 and Theiss Pty Ltd v Warren Brothers Earthmoving Pty Ltd & Ors [2012] QSC 373, the Court held that a “correction” cannot involve adopting a completely different method of calculation or chain of reasoning that produces materially different results.

If the adjudicator declines to correct, that is itself within jurisdiction. McDougall J in Musico v Davenport:

It might be thought that, in the ordinary case, a proper exercise of the discretion conferred by s22 (5) (s28 BCIPA) would favour the correction of mistakes of the kind referred to therein. However, it is a matter for the adjudicator whether or not any such correction is to be made. If an adjudicator declines to make any correction — for example, because he or she thinks there is no mistake — then the error (if any) will be an error within jurisdiction.

The touchstone is inadvertence: Cawood v Infraworth Pty Ltd [1990] 2 Qd R 114 at 122 (Macrossan CJ): “Inadvertence, as distinguished from an error or mistake resulting from deliberate decision, is the basis of the jurisdiction to correct under the slip rule”. Hansen Yuncken Pty Ltd & Anor v Yuanda Australia Pty Ltd & Anor [2018] SASC 158 (Lovell J) confirms that a refusal to exercise the slip rule is within jurisdiction and not reviewable.

Practice tips

  • Treat the adjudication as “pay now, fight later”. Even where you have strong grounds, plan the cash response (security, bond, draw on retention) before you plan the legal response — the courts repeatedly say they will not readily set aside an adjudication.
  • Build the payment schedule like a pleading. Every reason you might want the adjudicator to consider must already be there. New reasons in the response are excluded by s88(3)(b) and the cases applying s82 (Total Lifestyle Windows [2021] QSC 92; Chevron Park [2024] QSC 202; Lipman [2025] NSWSC 865). If the schedule says “Nil” without reasons, expect the claim to be paid in full (Insite [2023] QSC 33).
  • Tie every value to a specific item of construction work or related goods and services if you ever want to rely on s87 to bind a later adjudicator. Liquidated damages, set-offs and global delay costs typically will not attract s87 (O’Brien; Goyder Wind Farm; Harlech).
  • Do not rely on a court to fix an adjudicator’s contract-interpretation error. Misconstruing the contract is almost always within jurisdiction (Caltex Refineries; Laing O’Rourke v Samsung). The decision is set aside only if the adjudicator stops applying the contract entirely or interprets it so unreasonably no reasonable person could have done so.
  • If the adjudicator decides on a basis neither side argued — a clause not raised, a reference date not contended for, an interpretation no one canvassed — flag a denial of natural justice immediately and identify what you would have said. The threshold is something that “might well persuade the adjudicator to change his or her mind”: Trysams; Northbuild v Beyfield.
  • Do not pin a challenge on inadequate or sparse reasons. Brevity is not the same as failure to engage (Avopiling; 1155 Nepean Highway; Cockram Construction; Iskra; Kuatro Build). The recent NSW Court of Appeal authority — Ceerose and Martinus Rail — has narrowed the runway considerably.
  • Consider severance under s101(4) before declaring a whole determination dead. The court can save the parts not affected by jurisdictional error (Sunshine Coast Regional Council v Earthpro; Karam Group) and remittal is increasingly unlikely (Heavy Plant Leasing; Total Lifestyle Windows (No. 2)).
  • Use s89 properly: if there is a genuine clerical slip or material miscalculation in the decision, write to the adjudicator quickly and identify the four-corner basis. Do not dress up a substantive disagreement as a slip — the adjudicator cannot redo their reasoning, and refusal to correct is itself within jurisdiction (Uniting Church; Theiss; Hansen Yuncken v Yuanda).

Chapter 3 · Sections 90–93

Enforcement

Sections 90 to 93 turn an adjudicator’s decision into something with real teeth. Section 90 fixes the date by which the respondent must pay. Section 91 produces the adjudication certificate — the document the registrar issues that records the debt. Section 92 unlocks the right to suspend work for non-payment under s98. Section 93 is the conversion mechanism: filed in court with the right affidavit, the certificate becomes a judgment debt that can be enforced like any other, and the respondent’s ability to fight back is sharply curtailed. For a PM, this page is the playbook for moving from a paper win to money in the bank, and for understanding what an unhappy respondent can — and cannot — do about it.

Section 90 — respondent required to pay the adjudicated amount

Section 90 sets the statutory due date for payment of the adjudicated amount. Missing the date is not just a contractual default — it carries a penalty and is also a disciplinary matter under the QBCC Act.

Section 90 BIF Act – Respondent required to pay adjudicated amount
90 Respondent required to pay adjudicated amount

If an adjudicator decides that a respondent is required to pay an adjudicated amount, the respondent must pay the amount to the claimant on or before—

the day that is 5 business days after the day on which a copy of the adjudicator’s decision is given to the respondent by the adjudicator; or

if the adjudicator decides a later date for payment under section 88(1)(b)—the later date.

Maximum penalty—200 penalty units.

Note—

A failure to pay an adjudicated amount on or before the due date is also a ground for taking disciplinary action under the Queensland Building and Construction Commission Act 1991.

Section 91 — the adjudication certificate

If the respondent has been ordered to pay, the registrar must issue an adjudication certificate as soon as practicable, and no later than five business days after the decision. The certificate is the document the claimant needs to convert the decision into a judgment under s93. The registrar will not issue a certificate where there is nothing to enforce — for example where the respondent owes nothing under the decision, or where the adjudicator declined jurisdiction or found the application frivolous or vexatious.

Section 91 BIF Act – Adjudication certificate
91 Adjudication certificate

As soon as practicable after being given a copy of a decision by an adjudicator, but no later than 5 business days after being given the decision, the registrar must give the claimant a certificate (an adjudication certificate) of the decision stating the following matters—

the name of the claimant;

the name of the respondent who is liable to pay the adjudicated amount;

the adjudicated amount;

the date on which payment of the adjudicated amount was required to be paid to the claimant;

the rate of interest payable on the adjudicated amount;

the fees, identified in the decision, that the respondent is to pay;

that the certificate is made under this Act.

However, the registrar does not have to give the claimant an adjudication certificate if—

the respondent is not required to pay an amount under the decision; or

the adjudicator decided he or she does not have jurisdiction to decide the application; or

the adjudicator decided the adjudication application is frivolous or vexatious.

Section 92 — consequence of not paying: the right to suspend

If the adjudicated amount is not paid in full by the s90 due date, the claimant can put the respondent on notice that it intends to suspend work or supply under s98. The notice must say it is given under this Act. Suspension under s98 is the most powerful self-help tool the claimant has short of court enforcement — a properly served s92 notice is the trigger.

Section 92 BIF Act – Consequence of not paying adjudicated amount
92 Consequence of not paying adjudicated amount

If the respondent fails to pay the whole or any part of the adjudicated amount to the claimant as required under section 90, the claimant may give the respondent written notice of the claimant’s intention to suspend carrying out construction work, or supplying related goods and services, under the construction contract under section 98.

The notice about suspending work must state that it is made under this Act.

Section 93 — filing the adjudication certificate as a judgment debt

Section 93 is the mechanism that gives the regime its commercial bite. Once filed in a court of competent jurisdiction with the right affidavit, the certificate is treated as a judgment for a debt and can be enforced as such. Critically, if the respondent tries to set the judgment aside, s93(4) shuts the door on counterclaims, contractual defences and challenges to the adjudicator’s decision — and forces the unpaid portion into court as security in the meantime.

Section 93 BIF Act – Filing of adjudication certificate as judgment debt
93 Filing of adjudication certificate as judgment debt

An adjudication certificate may be filed as a judgment for a debt, and may be enforced, in a court of competent jurisdiction.

An adjudication certificate can not be filed under this section unless it is accompanied by an affidavit by the claimant stating that the whole or a part of the adjudicated amount has not been paid to the claimant at the time the certificate is filed.

If the affidavit states that part of the adjudicated amount has been paid, the judgment is for the unpaid part of the amount only.

If the respondent commences proceedings to have the judgment set aside—

the respondent is not, in those proceedings, entitled—

to bring any counterclaim against the claimant; or

to raise any defence in relation to matters arising under the construction contract to which the adjudication certificate relates; or

to challenge the adjudicator’s decision; and

the respondent is required to pay into the court, as security, the unpaid portion of the adjudicated amount pending the final decision in those proceedings.

93.1 — injunctions to restrain filing or enforcement

Where a respondent wants to head off enforcement, the usual route is to apply for an interlocutory injunction restraining the claimant from filing the certificate (or enforcing the resulting judgment). The framework comes from Australian Broadcasting Corporation v O’Neill [2006] HCA 46; (2006) 227 CLR 57, which requires the applicant to establish:

  • a prima facie case; and
  • that the balance of convenience favours the granting of an injunction.

“Prima facie case” here does not mean “more probable than not”:

it is sufficient that the plaintiff show a sufficient likelihood of success to justify in the circumstances the preservation of the status quo pending the trial.

On balance of convenience, the legislative policy of the Act — preserving cash flow to builders — is itself a relevant factor: see R J Neller Building Pty Ltd v Ainsworth [2008] QCA 397; [2009] 1 Qd R 390; Wiggins Island Coal Export Terminal Pty Ltd v Sun Engineering (Qld) Pty Ltd [2014] QSC 170; BRB Modular Pty Ltd v AWX Constructions Pty Ltd & Ors [2015] QSC 222.

The leading Queensland authority is R J Neller Building Pty Ltd v Ainsworth [2008] QCA 397; [2009] 1 Qd R 390. The Court of Appeal held that the legislature has assigned the risk that a builder may not be able to refund money later found to be due to the owner. Per Keane JA (Fraser JA and Fryberg J agreeing):

It is evidently the intention of the BCIP Act, and, in particular, s 31 and s 100 to which reference has been made, that the process of adjudication established under that Act should provide a speedy and effective means of ensuring cash flow to builders from the parties with whom they contract, where those parties operate in a commercial, as opposed to a domestic, context. This intention reflects an appreciation on the part of the legislature that an assured cash flow is essential to the commercial survival of builders, and that if a payment the subject of an adjudication is withheld pending the final resolution of the builder’s entitlement to the payment, the builder may be ruined.

The BCIP Act proceeds on the assumption that the interruption of a builder’s cash flow may cause the financial failure of the builder before the rights and wrongs of claim and counterclaim between builder and owner can be finally determined by the courts. On that assumption, the BCIP Act seeks to preserve the cash flow to a builder notwithstanding the risk that the builder might ultimately be required to refund the cash in circumstances where the builder’s financial failure, and inability to repay, could be expected to eventuate. Accordingly, the risk that a builder might not be able to refund moneys ultimately found to be due to a non-residential owner after a successful action by the owner must, I think, be regarded as a risk which, as a matter of policy in the commercial context in which the BCIP Act applies, the legislature has, prima facie at least, assigned to the owner.

Neller has been applied beyond Queensland. In Parkview Constructions Pty Limited v Total Lifestyle Windows Pty Ltd [2016] NSWSC 1911, Slattery J cited R J Neller in considering an injunction restraining the claimant from applying for or filing an adjudication certificate. The underlying issue was whether the adjudicator had erred in finding the response was out of time. On the threshold question, his Honour held:

In this case, it does seem to me that there is a serious factual issue to be tried. It is a simple issue, but one which will have decisive consequences for each side depending on how it is decided. If Parkview does succeed in showing that delivery of this application to it occurred on the 10th November, then it has a strong prospects of success. If on the other hand the adjudicator’s determination is upheld as a matter of fact, then Windows has sound chances of success, notwithstanding the alternative legal arguments that have been deployed.

On balance of convenience, Slattery J held:

In my view the balance of convenience favours the payment of these funds into Court rather than their payment to Windows in accordance with the filed adjudication certificate. This is principally because the factual issue in the case is so simple that it can very quickly be readied for hearing on both sides and be heard very early in the new term…

His Honour granted the interlocutory injunction restraining the claimant from filing the certificate.

In Low v MCC Pty Ltd & Ors; MCC Pty Ltd v Low [2018] QSC 6, Jackson J restrained MCC from enforcing two adjudication certificates and the warrants issued under them, on the basis that:

  • there was a serious question to be tried as to whether the payment claim, the subject of each adjudication, was served in respect of a valid reference date; and
  • the balance of convenience favoured granting the injunction, particularly because Low had given an undertaking to lodge a bank guarantee with the registrar of the court as security for the unpaid portion of the adjudicated amount pending the final decision of the Court regarding the validity of the adjudicator’s decision.

The doubt about whether a valid reference date had accrued was enough to amount to a serious question; the bank guarantee undertaking, combined with concerns about MCC’s ability to repay, swung the balance of convenience.

The relationship between R J Neller and post-Kirk jurisdictional error case law has been litigated. In Wiggins Island Coal Export Terminal Pty Ltd v Sun Engineering (Qld) Pty Ltd [2014] QSC 170, Daubney J considered R J Neller in light of Kirk v Industrial Court of New South Wales [2010] HCA 1; (2010) 239 CLR 531, and concluded that nothing in Kirk displaced the legislative policy on apportionment of risk identified in Neller. That approach was followed in Sunshine Coast Regional Council v Earthpro Pty Ltd & Ors [2014] QSC 271, [15], where Peter Lyons J characterised the relevant level of repayment risk as “substantial risk”: at [23].

However, Wiggins Island was doubted and not followed in BRB Modular Pty Ltd v AWX Constructions Pty Ltd & Ors [2015] QSC 222. Bond J drew a distinction between valid adjudication decisions and decisions affected by jurisdictional error, holding that a prima facie case of jurisdictional error is a relevant matter on the injunction analysis:

The intention of the Act – and in particular the intention revealed in ss 31 and 100 of the Act, to which Keane JA adverted in R J Neller at [39] – must be taken to be an intention revealed in relation to decisions of an adjudicator, made within jurisdiction. The policy of the Act described by Keane JA in R J Neller only applies in its full force to valid adjudication decisions. To my mind, it could not possibly be intended to be the policy of the Act that the risk is so passed in relation to decisions which are void and liable to be quashed by the Courts.

It follows that I respectfully disagree with the approach taken by Daubney J in the WICET decision. It seems to me that the appropriate analytical significance of the Act’s policy in cases such as the present is that the policy characterises the nature of the right that is in question in the interlocutory injunction application. So if, on an interlocutory injunction application of this nature, the applicant has established a prima facie case that the adjudication decision is void for jurisdictional error, in considering the question which arises on the balance of convenience, in considering where the lower risk lies, it is appropriate to consider the fact that granting an injunction would deny to someone a right, the nature of which is that described by Keane JA in R J Neller. The way in which this plays out in the exercise of the discretion is in the balancing process described by Hansen J in Kellogg Brown & Root Pty Ltd.

NSW and the ACT have taken a similar position: where a prima facie case (or serious issue to be tried) is shown, courts will grant an interlocutory injunction but typically only on the condition that the disputed amount be paid into court (or an agreed managed fund) pending final determination — see Filadelfia Projects Pty Limited v EntirlTy Business Services [2010] NSWSC 473; Nazero Group Pty Limited v Top Quality Construction Pty Ltd [2015] NSWSC 232; Milburn Lake Pty Ltd v Andritz Pty Ltd [2016] VSC 3. Where a party cannot presently make payment in, an undertaking to do so may be sufficient: Filadelfia Projects Pty Limited v EntirlTy Business Services [2010] NSWSC 473.

In Hakea Holdings Pty Limited v Denham Constructions Pty Ltd; BaptistCare NSW & ACT v Denham Constructions Pty Ltd [2016] NSWSC 1120, Ball J continued an injunction preventing the respondent from enforcing an adjudication determination. The applicants had established prima facie cases that they did not owe the amounts under their respective contracts, and there was little prospect of recovering any amount paid to the respondent given its current and future solvency. Those factors strongly favoured a stay and continuation of the injunction.

In Superb Build Pty Ltd v Petrosyan [2023] NSWDC 2, Andronos J extended an interim stay on a judgment giving effect to an adjudication determination, applying the Hakea factors. The matters that drove the result were:

  • the strength of the Respondent’s case;
  • significant risk of irreparable financial harm to the Respondent if the stay was not extended; and
  • no evidence that the Claimant would become insolvent if the interim stay was extended.

In Richard Crookes Constructions Pty Ltd v CES Projects (Aust) Pty Ltd [2016] NSWSC 1119, McDougall J held the adjudicator had arguably failed to perform his statutory function of assessing the construction work performed and valuing it. There was a prima facie case for relief and the balance of convenience favoured an injunction restraining enforcement until final hearing.

The contrary line is also strong. In Bellerive Homes Pty Ltd v FW Projects Pty Ltd [2018] NSWSC 1435, N Adams J refused a stay of judgment because granting it would have cut across the policy of prompt payment. Her Honour treated stays as discretionary and fact-specific, and gave the following examples of when one might be granted:

  • Failure to order a stay would “convert an amount which ought to be an interim payment into a final payment” (i.e. where there is a certainty that the party against who the stay is sought will suffer irreparable prejudice).
  • A respondent can demonstrate that the result produced by the NSW Act is unjust and there is a substantial risk the money paid over would be irrecoverable and that proceedings for a final resolution are being pursued.
  • The builder may have engaged in tactics calculated to delay the ultimate determination of the rights and liabilities of the parties so as to unfairly increase the owner’s exposure to the risk the builder’s insolvency.
  • The builder may have restructured its finances after the making of the contract to increase the risk to the owner of the possible inability of the builder to meet its liabilities to the owner when they are ultimately declared by the courts.

Concerns about the claimant’s future income stream were not enough on their own to justify a stay.

That same reluctance to interfere appears in Paul Kennedy McCullough trading as The Green Side Up v New Start Australia Pty Limited [2023] NSWDC 82, where Dicker SC DCJ held that a court should not stay enforcement of an adjudication certificate, where “the whole purpose of the (Security of Payment) Act would… be circumvented if the adjudication certificate was not given proper force consistently with its registration in accordance with s 25” of the NSW SOP Act (equivalent to s93 BIFSOPA), unless “failure to do so would [make] permanent that which the Act intended to be only interim” and “there is a likelihood of ‘irreparable prejudice’…”, referring to Veolia Water Solutions & Technologies (Australia) Pty Ltd v Kruger Engineering Australia Pty Ltd (No 3) [2007] NSWSC 459.

In Kuatro Build Pty Ltd v Elite Formwork Group Pty Ltd [2025] NSWSC 372, Hmelnitsky J held that, although interlocutory orders had initially restrained enforcement under s24(1)(a), the stay should not be continued (relevant to s92(1) BIFSOPA). His Honour applied Martinus Rail Pty Ltd v Qube RE Services (No 2) [2024] NSWSC 1223 and Martinus [2025] NSWCA 49, reiterating that “considerable caution should attend the grant of such an injunction or a stay,” as it risks undermining the SOP Act’s policy of immediate cash flow. Elite’s financial evidence, including evidence of trading viability and tax repayment capacity, was unchallenged, and Kuatro’s insolvency concerns were insufficient to maintain the stay.

On enforcement procedure, in Fitz Jersey Pty Ltd v Atlas Construction Group Pty Ltd [2017] NSWCA 53, Beazley ACJ, Basten JA and Leeming JA considered s25(4) of the BCIPA (NSW), the equivalent of s19 of the Act, and addressed whether an affected party must be given notice of a judgment debt before enforcement steps are taken. The Court held a judgment can be enforced without notice; nothing in the Act required the builder to notify the developer of garnishee proceedings. Basten J reasoned at [70]:

Although s25(4) of the Act is said not to confer any right to have a judgment set aside, the developer’s claim was that it recognized an entitlement which otherwise existed to have the judgment set aside. It would be incorrect to suggest that s25 (4) put the respondent to a claim in any better position than an unsuccessful respondent against whom a judgment debt has been obtained in civil proceedings in a court.

On the question of what counts as the “unpaid portion” of the adjudicated amount, in Kennedy Civil Contracting Pty Ltd (subject to Deed of Company Arrangement) v Linx Constructions Pty Ltd [2024] NSWSC 366, Wright J held that a payment to a third party could be taken into account in determining the unpaid portion under s15(2)(a)(i) of the SOP Act (equivalent to s93(3) BIFSOPA). The Magistrate had treated this as “simply a factual determination” that the third-party payment reduced the amount outstanding under the payment claim. Wright J also rejected the argument that such payment was not made “to the claimant” within s14(4) of the SOP Act (equivalent to s77(2) BIFSOPA), and refused leave to appeal on the basis that the unpaid portion was a question of fact or mixed fact and law.

That outcome was upheld in Kennedy Civil Contracting Pty Ltd v Linx Constructions Pty Ltd [2024] NSWCA 243, where the Court of Appeal held that a respondent’s payment to a third-party supplier did not constitute a cross-claim under s15(4)(b)(ii) of the NSW Act (equivalent to s93 BIFSOPA). The respondent had failed to issue a payment schedule in response to a $147,000 payment claim that included approximately $78,000 for goods and services supplied by a third party, and had subsequently paid $102,800 in total. The Magistrate found the $30,000 direct payment to the third party had partially satisfied the debt, and the Court of Appeal affirmed that conclusion: while the Act strictly limits defences where no payment schedule is given, reductions based on accepted third-party payments may be considered in limited, fact-specific circumstances.

93.2 — statutory demands

Adjudicated amounts are also routinely used as the basis for statutory demands under s459H of the Corporations Act 2001 (Cth). The current authority in this context is Re Douglas Aerospace Pty Ltd [2015] NSWSC 167, which held that:

  • a genuine dispute is not founded on an application to set aside the debt; but
  • an offsetting claim can exist on the presence of a counterclaim, set-off or cross demand that does not deny the debt, but asserts a countervailing liability.

That approach has not been universally embraced. In Re J Group Constructions Pty Ltd [2015] NSWSC 1607, Robb J reviewed the line of authority running through Diploma Construction (WA) Pty Ltd v KPA Architects Pty Ltd [2014] WASCA 91 and Re Douglas Aerospace Pty Ltd [2015] NSWSC 167, ultimately following Re Douglas Aerospace, but flagging “reasons for doubting” the application of the principles in Deputy Commissioner of Taxation v Broadbeach Properties Pty Ltd [2008] HCA 41; (2008) 237 CLR 473 to security-of-payment debts — given the different nature of those debts compared with the tax debts in Broadbeach.

In In the matter of Rockwall Homes Pty Limited [2017] NSWSC 223, Black J considered whether ss15(2) and (4) of the NSW Act prevented a company served with a creditors statutory demand from raising a genuine dispute under s459G of the Corporations Act. His Honour referred to Brereton J’s reasoning in Re Douglas Aerospace at [76]:

some debts are not capable of genuine dispute — because they are judgment debts or conclusive debts — but may still be amenable to an offsetting claim. It is difficult to see how a debt arising under s 14(4) (“SOPA”), which creates a statutory liability upon the failure to provide a payment schedule, could be the subject of a genuine dispute, if the conditions in s 15(1) are satisfied, regardless of any underlying dispute. As it seems to me, the only way in which a “genuine dispute” could be raised in respect of such a debt would be by disputing whether the circumstances referred to in s 15(1) existed.

Black J found that reasoning did not apply to the claim before him: the SOP Act only operates where a payment claim has been served on a party to a construction contract, and a dispute about whether that requirement is satisfied is not excluded. His Honour also referred to CGI Information Systems & Management Consultants Pty Ltd v APRA Consulting Pty Ltd [2003] NSWSC 728, where Barrett J observed:

“[T]he task faced by the company challenging a statutory demand on the genuine dispute grounds is by no means at all a difficult or demanding one. The company will fail in that task only if it is found, upon the hearing of its s 459G application, that the contentions upon which it seeks to rely in mounting its challenge are so devoid of substance that no further investigation is warranted. Once the company shows that even one issue has a sufficient degree of cogency to be arguable, a finding of genuine dispute must follow. The court does not engage in any form of balancing exercise between the strengths of competing contentions. If it sees any factor that on rational grounds indicates an arguable case on the part of the company, it must find that a genuine dispute exists, even where any case apparently available to be advanced against the company seems stronger.”

Form matters as well as substance. In Re Roberts Construction Group Pty Ltd [2024] VSC 679, Steffensen AsJ set aside a statutory demand issued in respect of an unpaid scheduled amount under the Victorian SOP Act because the demand did not adequately describe the legal basis of the debt — it did not refer to the “scheduled amount”, the document under which it arose, or the SOP Act. Her Honour relied on Re Three Pillars Lynbrook Pty Ltd [2022] VSC 540 (a statutory demand must be unambiguous) and CM Luxury Pty Ltd v Menzies Civil Australia Pty Ltd [2023] WASC 340 (a statutory demand must accurately describe the legal basis of the debt or it can cause substantial injustice). The takeaway is that statutory demands relying on SOP debts must expressly state the basis of the demand.

93.3 — challenging an adjudication determination

A respondent who wants to set aside the s93 judgment must usually do so by attacking the underlying adjudication for jurisdictional error. In Amasya Enterprises Pty Ltd & Anor v Asta Developments (Aust) Pty Ltd & Anor [2015] VSC 233, Vickery J considered s28R(5)(a)(iii) of the VIC Act (equivalent to s31(4)(a)(iii) of the Act) where the plaintiffs sought to challenge the adjudicator’s determination, and held:

… if the Plaintiffs are successful in a challenge to the Adjudicator’s Determination founded upon jurisdictional error, the Supreme Court may make an order in the nature of certiorari or a declaration to remove the legal consequences or purported legal consequences of the Adjudication Determination under the Act, with the result that the judgment founded upon it, must also be set aside.

In arriving at that conclusion, his Honour stated:

…the operation of the privative clause in s 28R(5)(a)(iii) is confined to denying relief being granted by a court in Victoria, including the Supreme Court, in the course of a proceeding to set aside a judgment entered pursuant to s 28R, where the error relied upon is an error on the face of the record in an adjudication determination which is the foundation of the judgment. In other words, pursuant to s 28R(5)(a)(iii) of the Act, it is not open to challenge an adjudication determination (or a review determination) in a proceeding to have a s 28R judgment set aside, on the basis or an error on the face of the record in the relevant determination.

It follows in this case that, if the Plaintiffs are able to establish a jurisdictional error in the Adjudication Determination, they are not precluded by the operation of s 28R(5)(a)(iii) of the Act from challenging the Adjudication Determination on that basis.

93.4 — section 93(4)(a): no counterclaim, no contract defence, no challenge

Section 93(4)(a) is the lock on the door. In set-aside proceedings, the respondent cannot bring a counterclaim, raise contract defences, or challenge the adjudicator’s decision. The cases below show how the courts have policed those limits.

In Infinity Formwork Group Pty Ltd v Adobe Construction Group Pty Ltd [2023] NSWDC 220, the Court considered whether equitable set-off fell within s15(4)(b)(ii) of the NSW SOP Act (equivalent to s93(4)(a)(ii) BIFSOPA) and was therefore excluded as a defence to a summary judgment application. The respondent had failed to provide a payment schedule. Adronos SC DCJ held that equitable set-off was caught by the exclusion and was not available, stating that it was “incumbent on the defendant, if it wished to rely on the defence, to raise it in a payment schedule”. Summary judgment for the full amount followed.

The line is not absolute. In Marques Group Pty Ltd v Parkview Constructions Pty Ltd [2023] NSWSC 625, Rees J considered a respondent’s defence to summary judgment under s16(2)(a)(i) of the NSW SOP Act for failure to pay a scheduled amount. Sections 15(4)(b) and 16(4)(b) (equivalent to s93(4)(a) BIFSOPA) preclude crossclaims and defences in relation to matters arising under the construction contract. Applying Bitannia Pty Ltd v Parkline Constructions Pty Ltd (2006) 67 NSWLR 9, Rees J held that a misleading and deceptive conduct argument under the ACL is not a defence in relation to matters arising under the contract, so the respondent was not precluded from running it. The defence was “arguable”, and summary judgment was refused.

In Kennedy Civil Contracting Pty Ltd (subject to Deed of Company Arrangement) v Linx Constructions Pty Ltd [2024] NSWSC 366, Wright J held that the Respondent’s reliance on a payment to a third party to reduce the “unpaid portion of the claimed amount” did not amount to an impermissible cross-claim within s15(4)(b)(i) of the SOP Act (equivalent to s93(4)(a)(i) BIFSOPA). At first instance, the Magistrate had rejected the cross-claim characterisation and reasoned that the Claimant should not be entitled to be paid an amount it never intended to retain. The Court emphasised that the Respondent had not advanced a claim for independent or additional relief that could be given in separate proceedings — which is inherent in a cross-claim. The payment was a factual contention going to the level of debt outstanding under the payment claim.

Where the underlying determination is void for jurisdictional error, the s93(4) restrictions do not save the resulting judgment. In Harlech Enterprises Pty Ltd v Beno Excavations Pty Ltd t/as Benex Pipeline [2024] NSWDC 151, Montgomery DCJ considered whether to set aside a 2021 judgment and garnishee order based on an adjudication determination later quashed by the ACT Supreme Court for jurisdictional error in Beno Excavations Pty Ltd v Harlech Enterprises (No 2) [2021] ACTSC 269 (Mossop J). His Honour applied Brodyn Pty Ltd t/as Time Cost and Quality v Davenport & Anor [2004] NSWCA 394, citing [61]:

…until it [adjudication determination] is filed, proceedings can appropriately be brought in a court with jurisdiction to grant declarations and injunctions to establish that it is void and to prevent it being filed. However, once it has been filed, the resulting judgement is not void. An application can be made to set aside the judgement… it is not contrary to s 25(4)(a)(iii) to do so on the basis that there is in truth no adjudicator’s determination.

Montgomery DCJ found it was not contrary to s25(4)(a)(iii) of the SOP Act (NSW) (equivalent to s93(4)(iii) BIFSOPA) to set aside the judgment on that basis: the statutory scheme did not contemplate enforcement of a judgment debt where the determination was infected with jurisdictional error. The judgment and garnishee order were set aside.

The set-off line was tested again in BRP Industries Pty Ltd v Hynash Constructions Pty Ltd [2024] NSWDC 392. The Claimant applied for summary judgment for $234,286.80 where the Respondent had failed to issue a payment schedule. The Respondent argued it had paid a $110,756.80 upfront amount and that the debt should be reduced by that figure. The issue was whether that argument was a defence “in relation to matters arising under the construction contract” for the purposes of s15(4)(b)(ii) of the NSW Act (equivalent to s93(4)(ii) BIFSOPA). Russell SC DCJ held the set-off could not be raised: the contract required a 10% upfront establishment and mobilisation fee, and the $110,756.80 was that fee. Because the obligation to make the upfront payment arose under the contract, the set-off was a defence in relation to a matter arising under the contract and was barred.

That decision was upheld in Hynash Constructions Pty Ltd v BRP Industries Pty Ltd [2025] NSWCA 14, where the NSW Court of Appeal held that the “unpaid portion” in s15(2)(a)(i) of the NSW Act refers to amounts unpaid in response to the payment claim, not unrelated prior payments. If a respondent wants a prior payment credited, it must be raised in the payment schedule.

Finally, in Martinus Rail Pty Ltd v Qube RE Services (No.2) Pty Ltd [2025] NSWCA 49, Payne JA confirmed that entry of judgment under s25 (equivalent to s93(1) BIFSOPA) is not precluded by a pending arbitration or by the mere possibility of repayment risk. The Court upheld the primacy of the SOP Act’s enforcement mechanism, rejecting Qube’s argument that enforcement should be stayed pending a separate arbitration. Adjudicated amounts became payable by the statutory due date and “the grant of a stay until the end of arbitration would be contrary to the statutory purpose of the SOP Act.” The Court applied Kennedy Civil Contracting Pty Ltd v Richard Crookes Construction Pty Ltd [2023] NSWSC 99 and Probuild in affirming the swift enforcement intended under s25.

Practice tips

  • Diary the s90 due date the moment the decision lands — five business days from delivery, unless the adjudicator has set a later date under s88(1)(b). Missing the date triggers a 200 penalty unit exposure and is a QBCC disciplinary trigger.
  • If you are the claimant and you have not been paid by the due date, get the adjudication certificate from the registrar (s91) and prepare the s93 affidavit confirming non-payment (or partial payment) before filing — the certificate cannot be filed without it.
  • If you intend to suspend under s98, serve a written s92 notice that expressly says it is given under the BIF Act — that statement is mandatory and is the gateway to the s98 right.
  • If you are the respondent and want to restrain filing or enforcement, expect the court to require payment of the unpaid portion into court (or an undertaking to do so). Plan the cash — R J Neller, Low v MCC and the NSW line all point the same way.
  • In set-aside proceedings, do not waste time on contractual defences, counterclaims or merits challenges to the decision — s93(4)(a) shuts those out. Focus on jurisdictional error or, in narrow cases, ACL/statutory arguments that are not “defences in relation to matters arising under the contract”.
  • If you are running a statutory demand off an adjudicated debt, draft it carefully: identify the scheduled or adjudicated amount, the underlying document and the SOP Act basis. Vague demands have been set aside (Re Roberts Construction Group).

Chapter 3 · Sections 94–98

Other rights and suspension of work

Sections 94 to 98 of the BIF Act deal with what happens around the edges of an adjudication: when an adjudicator runs out of time, how the adjudicator is paid, who bears the cost, how an application is brought to an end, and the claimant’s right to down tools while waiting to be paid. For a project manager, these provisions matter because they determine whether you can keep the security-of-payment process moving when something goes wrong, and they create a powerful self-help remedy — lawful suspension — that can be deployed without going to court.

Section 94 – Claimant may make a new application

If an adjudicator accepts a referral but does not deliver a decision within the time required by section 85, the claimant is not stuck. Section 94 gives the claimant a fresh choice: ask the registrar to refer the original application to another adjudicator, or start over by lodging a new application under section 79.

Section 94 BIF Act – Claimant may make new application in certain circumstances
94 Claimant may make new application in certain circumstances

(1)  Subsection (2) applies if an adjudicator, who accepts a referral to decide an adjudication application (the original application), does not decide the application within the period required under section 85.

(2)  The claimant may do either of the following within 5 business days after the period mentioned in subsection (1)—

(a)  request the registrar refer the original application to another adjudicator; or

(b)  make a new adjudication application (the new application) under section 79.

(3)  If the claimant requests the registrar refer the original application to another adjudicator—

(a)  the registrar must refer the application to another adjudicator within 4 business days after the request is made; and

(b)  no fee is payable for referring the original application to another adjudicator.

(4)  Also, if another adjudicator accepts the referral, this division applies for the original application with the following changes—

(a)  the claimant must give the adjudicator a copy of his or her submissions included in the original application within 5 business days after the adjudicator accepts the referral;

(b)  the respondent must give the adjudicator a copy of his or her submissions included in an adjudication response, if any, for the original application within 5 business days after the adjudicator accepts the referral;

(c)  the response date for the application under section 85(2) is the day—

(i)  the adjudicator receives the copy of the submission from the respondent; or

(ii)  if the respondent does not give the adjudicator the copy of the submission within the period mentioned in paragraph (b)—immediately after the end of the period;

(d)  in deciding the adjudication application, the adjudicator must not consider any submissions other than—

(i)  the submissions mentioned in paragraph (a) or (b); or

(ii)  further written submissions asked for by the adjudicator under section 84(2)(b).

(5)  This division applies to the new application in the same way it applies to any other adjudication application made under section 79.

(6)  However, the claimant may, despite section 79(2)(b), make the new application within 5 business days after the claimant becomes entitled to act under subsection (2).

An “exhaustive” definition of the right

The Queensland Court of Appeal has held that the equivalent BCIPA provision “exhaustively defines” a claimant’s right to make a new adjudication application based on an earlier payment claim: Spankie v James Trowse Constructions Pty Ltd [2010] QCA 355, [29] (Fraser JA, Holmes and Chesterman JJA agreeing); Spankie & Ors v James Trowse Constructions Pty Ltd & Ors [2010] QSC 336. Following the Amendment Act, regard should also be had to the effect of section 35B of BCIPA (equivalent to section 97 of the Act).

New application after a previous decision is set aside

Whether a claimant can launch a new application after the previous adjudication decision has been set aside for jurisdictional error or breach of natural justice is not entirely settled. The competing views are canvassed in Hansen Yuncken Pty Ltd v Ian James Ericson t/as Flea’s Concreting & Anor [2011] QSC 327, [139]–[144] (McMurdo J); Spankie v James Trowse Constructions Pty Ltd [2010] QCA 355, [29] (Fraser JA, Holmes and Chesterman JJA agreeing); and Cardinal Project Services Pty Ltd v Hanave Pty Ltd [2010] NSWSC 1367.

New application where the previous decision is void

The position is much clearer where the previous decision is void for being out of time. In Galaxy Developments Pty Ltd v Civil Contractors (Aust) Pty Ltd t/a CCA Winslow & Ors [2020] QSC 51, Dalton J held that the delivery of an adjudication decision outside the time limits in sections 85 and 86 of BIFSOPA gives rise to an immediate right in the claimant to bring a second adjudication application under section 94. The Court reasoned that the purpose of the BIF Act is to provide the claimant with “a prompt extra-curial decision on its progress claim; not a guarantee that its progress claim will be paid.”

That approach was reinforced in Sun Engineering (Qld) Pty Ltd v Registrar Appointed under section 150 of the Building Industry Fairness (Security of Payment) Act 2017 [2023] QSC 168, where Kelly J held that section 94(1) applies where the adjudicator has failed to make a decision within the allocated time. Kelly J focused on the words “if an adjudicator… does not decide the application within the period required under” section 85: once that condition is met, the claimant can request a new referral or make a fresh application under section 79.

Section 95 – Adjudicator’s fees

Adjudicators are paid for their work, but only if they actually deliver a decision in time. Section 95 sets out the entitlement, the joint and several liability of the parties, and the limited carve-outs from the “no decision, no fee” rule.

Section 95 BIF Act – Adjudicator’s fees
95 Adjudicator’s fees

(1)  An adjudicator is entitled to be paid for adjudicating an adjudication application—

(a)  the amount, by way of fees and expenses, agreed between the adjudicator and the parties to the adjudication; or

(b)  if no amount is agreed, the amount, for fees and expenses, that is reasonable having regard to the work done and expenses incurred by the adjudicator.

(2)  A regulation may prescribe the maximum amount for fees and expenses (the prescribed maximum) an adjudicator may be paid for adjudicating an adjudication application.

(3)  Despite subsection (1), an adjudicator is not entitled to be paid any amount that is more than the prescribed maximum.

(4)  The claimant and respondent are jointly and severally liable to pay the adjudicator’s fees and expenses.

(5)  The claimant and respondent are each liable to contribute to the payment of the adjudicator’s fees and expenses in equal proportions unless the adjudicator decides otherwise.

(6)  An adjudicator is not entitled to be paid any fees or expenses for adjudicating an adjudication application if the adjudicator fails to make a decision on the application.

(7)  An adjudicator does not fail to make a decision only because—

(a)  the adjudication application is withdrawn; or

(b)  the adjudicator decided he or she did not have jurisdiction to adjudicate the application; or

(c)  the adjudicator decided the application was frivolous or vexatious; or

(d)  the adjudicator refuses to communicate the adjudicator’s decision on an adjudication application until the adjudicator’s fees and expenses are paid.

(8)  Also, if a court finds that the adjudicator’s decision is void and unenforceable, the adjudicator is still entitled to be paid any fees or expenses for adjudicating the application if the adjudicator acted in good faith in adjudicating the application.

(9)  In this section—

adjudicating, an adjudication application, includes accepting, considering and deciding the application.

Statutory right to payment

The adjudicator’s entitlement to fees is statutory, not contractual. In Alucity Architectural Produce Supply Pty Ltd v Australian Solutions Centre; Alucity Architectural Produce Supply Pty Ltd v Paul J Hick [2016] NSWSC 608, Hammerschlag J dismissed a claim for restitution of fees said to have been paid for a total failure of consideration, observing in respect of section 28 of the NSW Act:

The relationship between the [applicant] on the one hand, and the authority and the adjudicator on the other, is statutory. The entitlement of the authority and the adjudicator and the authority to payment arises as a consequence of the [applicant] having invoked the procedures under the Act and because ss 28 and 29 give the authority and the adjudicator a statutory right to payment.

That entitlement, however, falls away if the adjudicator misses the statutory deadline. In Galaxy Developments Pty Ltd v Civil Contractors (Aust) Pty Ltd t/a CCA Winslow & Ors [2020] QSC 51, the Court held that an adjudicator who delivered a decision outside the timeframe in sections 85 and 86 was not entitled to any fees. Dalton J found that the adjudicator failed to act in good faith for the purposes of section 95(8) where, through his agent, he represented that the decision had been made within time when that was not the case.

On appeal in Civil Contractors (Aust) Pty Ltd v Galaxy Developments Pty Ltd & Ors; Jones v Galaxy Developments Pty Ltd & Ors [2021] QCA 10, the Queensland Court of Appeal held that the primary judge had erred in finding the adjudicator had acted in bad faith and made misrepresentations, because the adjudicator had not been afforded an opportunity to be heard on those allegations. Notwithstanding that error, the Court of Appeal upheld the conclusion that the adjudicator was not entitled to fees because the decision was not made within the timeframe required by the BIF Act. The Court confirmed that even if the determination had been valid, or if the parties had been willing and able to grant an extension, section 95(6) would still apply.

Section 96 – Deciding fees payable by claimant and respondent

Section 96 lets the adjudicator decide who pays what — both the proportion of the adjudicator’s own fees and expenses, and the proportion of the application fee for which the respondent must reimburse the claimant. In doing so the adjudicator must consider the parties’ conduct and may consider relative success, improper purpose, prospects of success, the respondent’s reasons for non-payment, withdrawal, and the time the adjudicator spent on discrete issues.

Section 96 BIF Act – Deciding fees payable by claimant and respondent
96 Deciding fees payable by claimant and respondent

(1)  When making a decision about an adjudication application, an adjudicator may also decide the following—

(a)  the proportion of the adjudicator’s fees and expenses to be paid by the claimant and respondent;

(b)  the proportion of the fee, payable for the adjudication application under section 79(2)(d), for which the respondent is to reimburse the claimant.

(2)  In making the decision, the adjudicator—

(a)  must consider the conduct of the claimant and respondent; and

(b)  may consider any of the following matters—

(i)  the relative success of the claimant or respondent in the adjudication;

(ii)  whether the claimant or respondent commenced or participated in the adjudication for an improper purpose;

(iii)  whether the claimant or respondent commenced or participated in the adjudication without reasonable prospects of success;

(iv)  the reasons given by the respondent for not making the progress payment the subject of the adjudication application;

(v)  whether an adjudication application is withdrawn;

(vi)  the services provided by the adjudicator in adjudicating the adjudication application, including the amount of time taken to consider discrete aspects of the amount claimed;

(vii)  another matter the adjudicator considers relevant in making the decision.

(3)  In this section—

adjudicating, an adjudication application, includes accepting, considering and deciding the application.

conduct, of the claimant and respondent, includes—

(a)  whether the claimant or respondent has acted unreasonably leading up to, or during, the adjudication; or

(b)  whether the respondent attempted to include new reasons in an adjudication response in contravention of section 82(4). new reasons see section 82(4).

Section 97 – Withdrawing from adjudication

An adjudication application can be ended in two ways: the claimant can serve a written notice of discontinuation, or the respondent can pay the amount stated in the payment claim — in which case the application is taken to have been withdrawn. The adjudicator’s entitlement to fees for work already done survives a withdrawal (see section 95).

Section 97 BIF Act – Withdrawing from adjudication
97 Withdrawing from adjudication

(1)  An adjudication application—

(a)  is withdrawn if the claimant has given a written notice of discontinuation to the adjudicator and respondent; or

(b)  is taken to have been withdrawn if the respondent has, before an adjudicator has decided the application, paid the claimant the amount stated in the payment claim the subject of the adjudication application.

Note—

Despite the withdrawal of an adjudication application an adjudicator is still entitled to be paid fees for considering the application, see section 95.

(2)  If subsection (1)(b) applies, the claimant must as soon as practicable inform the adjudicator that the adjudication application has been withdrawn because of payment.

Refusal to communicate the adjudicator’s decision and deemed withdrawal

In Karam Group Pty Ltd as Trustee for Karam (No. 1) Family Trust v HCA Queensland Pty Ltd & Ors [2022] QSC 290, Williams J held that an adjudicator will have failed to make a determination within the statutory timeframe if they fail to attend to all aspects specified in section 88 of BIFSOPA, including notifying the parties that the decision had been made. The adjudicator in that case made the decision in time, but did not notify the parties until after the time prescribed by section 85(1) had passed. The Court held the decision was invalid because it had not been made within the time required by the Act.

The deemed-withdrawal limb of section 97 was considered in Downer EDI Rail Pty Ltd v DT Infrastructure Pty Ltd [2025] QSC 212, where Treston J declared that an adjudication application was taken to be withdrawn under section 97(1)(b) because the respondent paid the sum for which the adjudicator’s decision was sought, even though it had not paid the total payment claim. Treston J emphasised that the process under the Act is intended to be fast and efficient. The words “the subject of the adjudication application” direct attention to the genuine issues in the payment claim, and it would be an onerous construction of the legislation if the adjudicator were required to consider matters not in dispute on the face of the application (in that case, some $8.7 million worth of claims) when the submissions on both sides had been confined to $681,445.37 worth. Treston J distinguished the reasoning of Hodgson JA in Coordinated Construction Co Pty Ltd v JM Hargreaves (NSW) Pty Ltd (2005) 63 NSWLR 385.

Section 98 – Claimant’s right to suspend work

Section 98 is the BIF Act’s self-help remedy. A claimant who has not been paid — and who has given the requisite notice of intention to suspend under section 78 (failure to pay scheduled amount) or section 92 (failure to pay an adjudicated amount) — can lawfully stop work two business days after that notice. The right runs until payment is received and continues for another three business days after payment.

Section 98 BIF Act – Claimant’s right to suspend work
98 Claimant’s right to suspend work

(1)  A claimant may suspend carrying out construction work, or supplying related goods and services, under a construction contract if at least 2 business days have passed since the claimant gave notice of intention to do so to the respondent under section 78 or 92.

(2)  The right conferred under subsection (1) exists until the day on which the claimant receives payment from the respondent of the amount mentioned in section 78(1) or 92(1), and continues for another 3 business days immediately following that day.

(3)  If, in exercising the right to suspend carrying out construction work or supplying related goods and services under a construction contract, the claimant incurs a loss or expense because the respondent removes any part of the work or supply from the contract, the respondent is liable to pay the claimant the amount of the loss or expense.

(4)  A claimant who suspends carrying out construction work, or supplying related goods and services under a construction contract under subsection (1) is not liable for any loss or damage suffered by the respondent, or by any person claiming through the respondent, because of the claimant not carrying out that work or not supplying those goods and services, during the suspension.

What counts as “payment”

The right to suspend ends only when the claimant has actually received payment. In Beckhaus Civil Pty Ltd v Brewarrina Shire Council No. 2 [2004] NSWSC 1160, Master Macready defined the verb “pay” in this context as follows:

To give (a person) what is due in discharge of a debt, or as a return for services done, or goods received, or in compensation for injury done; to remunerate, recompense.” In turn, ‘payment’ is defined as “[t]he action, or an act, of paying.” From this it can readily be concluded that the touchstone of the ordinary and natural meaning of the expressions ‘to pay’ and ‘receives payment’ in the present context is the act of a debtor transferring by way of satisfaction whatever is owed by him or her to a creditor.

…

Thus an analysis of both the general and specific purposes of the Act permits the court to identify an alternative construction to the ordinary meaning of ‘pay’ and ‘payment.’ That is that a claimant will not have ‘received payment’ pursuant to s 27(2), and that a respondent will still have failed ‘to pay’ pursuant to s 15(1)(b), in circumstances where the claimant is unable to obtain the benefit of that payment due to conditions, such as the provision of a secured bank guarantee, placed on its receipt. The adoption of such a construction does not constitute a ‘re-writing’ of ss 15 and 27 as proscribed by the dictum of Dawson J above, but is rather a departure from the ordinary meaning of ‘payment’ in circumstances where the context and purpose of its use demands such a modification.

How long the right to suspend lasts

In On Forbes Developments Pty Ltd v Chase Building Group (Canberra) Pty Ltd [2020] ACTSC 163, Crowe AJ held that, after an adjudication determination in favour of a claimant, the claimant’s right to suspend work continues until the respondent gives an unequivocal acknowledgement of the discharge of the adjudicated amount. The claimant in that case had suspended work under section 26(2)(b) of the ACT Act (equivalent to section 98(1) of BIFSOPA) following the respondent’s failure to pay the adjudicated amount. A subsequent expert determination under the construction contract concluded that the claimant in fact owed the respondent more than the adjudicated amount. The respondent argued the right to suspend ended on the date of the expert determination. Crowe AJ rejected that submission, holding that the right continued until either the outstanding amount was set aside or the claimant received an unequivocal communication acknowledging discharge of the payment claim, the adjudicated amount or, in this case, the provisional judgment debt. On the facts, constructive receipt of the adjudication amount being set aside occurred when consent orders were made on the day of the hearing, and the claimant’s right to suspend then ended three business days later.

Practice tips

  • If your adjudicator misses the section 85 deadline, diarise the 5-business-day window in section 94(2) and decide quickly whether to ask the registrar to refer the application to another adjudicator (no further fee) or to file a fresh application under section 79.
  • Before suspending under section 98, make sure a compliant notice of intention to suspend has actually gone out under section 78 or section 92, and do not down tools until at least 2 business days have passed from service of that notice.
  • Treat the right to suspend as continuing until you have actually received payment plus a further 3 business days — bank guarantees or conditional payments may not count as “payment” (see Beckhaus), and after an adjudication you are entitled to an unequivocal acknowledgement of discharge before the right ends (see On Forbes).
  • A valid suspension under section 98 protects the claimant from liability for the respondent’s loss or damage during the down-tools period, and entitles the claimant to recover loss or expense if the respondent uses the suspension as cover to remove work from scope — document any descoping in writing as it occurs.
  • If a respondent pays the amount in the payment claim that is the subject of the adjudication application before the decision issues, recognise that the application is taken to be withdrawn under section 97(1)(b) (see Downer EDI Rail) and notify the adjudicator as soon as practicable.
  • Remember that adjudicator fees are joint and several under section 95(4), and that under section 96 the adjudicator can shift the cost burden based on conduct — keep your adjudication conduct clean (no improper purpose, no late “new reasons” in a response) to avoid being loaded with the fees.

Chapter 3 · Sections 99–102

Civil proceedings & service

Sections 99 to 102 deal with the back end of the security-of-payment process: the warning notice that has to be given before suing for an unpaid claim, the limited grounds on which a respondent can defend recovery proceedings, the way Part 3 sits alongside the parties’ ordinary contractual rights, and how notices and documents are validly served. For a project manager, these are the rules that decide whether you can convert a payment claim or adjudicated amount into money in the bank, and whether the “pay now, argue later” outcome is final or just an interim allocation of cash flow.

Section 99 – Notice required before starting particular proceedings

Section 99 is the “second-chance” provision. Before a claimant can sue in court to recover an unpaid payment claim, it must give the respondent a warning notice in the approved form. The respondent then has a short window in which it can still pay before proceedings start.

Section 99 BIF Act – Notice required before starting particular proceedings
99 Notice requires before starting particular proceedings

(1)  This section applies if—

(a)  after being given a payment claim, the respondent fails to pay the amount stated in the claim on or before the due date for the progress payment to which the claim relates; and

(b)  because of the failure to pay, the claimant intends to start proceedings in a court to recover the unpaid portion of the amount owed to the claimant.

Note—

See section 78 for the claimants right to recover from a respondent an amount owed to the claimant.

(2)  Before taking the intended action, the claimant must give the respondent written notice (a warning notice), in the approved form, of the claimant’s intention to start the proceedings.

(3)  The claimant must not give the respondent the warning notice later than 20 business days after the due date for the progress payment.

(4)  The claimant must not take the intended action before the end of 5 business days after giving the respondent the warning notice.

(5)  The giving of a warning notice does not—

(a)  require the claimant to complete the action stated in the notice; or

(b)  prevent the claimant from taking different action to that stated in the notice.

Section 99(2) – the warning notice

The warning notice is more than a polite reminder — it is a jurisdictional precondition to the court action it foreshadows. The Victorian Supreme Court considered an analogous provision in Vinson v Neerim Properties Developments Pty Ltd [2016] VSC 321, where Vickery J held that an email which merely “reserved” the claimant’s rights under the Act did not satisfy the statutory notice requirement:

Neerim made its purported Adjudication Application under s 18(1)(b) of the Act. However, s 18(2) of the Act provides a bar to the making of an adjudication application to which sub-section 1(b) applies unless both of the mandatory requirements of paragraphs 18(2)(a) have been satisfied.

As to the requirements under s 18(2)(a) of the Act, Neerim’s email of 9 February 2016, whether or not it was provided to Ms Vinson within the time prescribed, manifestly failed to notify her of Neerim’s intention to apply for adjudication of the Payment Claim, as is required. The notice, such that it was, merely reserved the exercise of the company’s rights under the Act. This is insufficient, for the purposes of the Act, to amount to a notice that Neerim intended to apply for adjudication of its Payment Claim.

The object and purpose of a s 18(2)(a) notice is to enable the respondent to the payment claim to be given an opportunity under s 18(2)(b) to provide a payment schedule to the claimant within 2 business days after receiving the claimant’s notice provided under s 18(2)(a). This opportunity was denied to Ms Vinson by Neerim simply stating that it ‘reserved’ its rights.

For these reasons, Neerim failed to comply with an essential and obligatory requirement of s 18(2) of the Act,[6] and the purported notice was invalid for this purpose.

The warning notice also operates as the trigger for any “second-chance” payment schedule the respondent might want to put on. In Forte Sydney Construction v Lin Betty Building Group [2018] NSWSC 1429, McDougall J held that a payment schedule served out of time under section 14 of the NSW Act, but before the section 17(2)(a) notice (the equivalent of section 20A BCIPA, and section 99 of the Act), is not a valid response to the section 17(2)(a) notice — in other words, the second-chance payment schedule must be served after the warning notice is received. In that case:

  • Forte Sydney provided its payment schedule outside the required 10 business day timeframe and as such, Lin Betty was required to provide Forte Sydney a further opportunity to provide a valid payment schedule, under section 17(2)(a) of the NSW Act, prior to applying for adjudication of the payment claim.
  • Forte Sydney did not respond to the section 17(2)(a) notice and, at adjudication, the adjudicator awarded Lin Betty the majority of its claim.

Counsel for Forte Sydney argued that its earlier (out-of-time) payment schedule should be treated as the response to the section 17(2)(a) notice. McDougall J rejected that submission, focusing on the wording in section 17(2)(b) of the NSW Act that the payment schedule must be provided “within 5 business days after receiving the claimant’s notice”. Because Forte Sydney’s purported payment schedule was issued before the notice was received, it could not have been provided “within 5 business days after receiving the claimant’s notice”.

Section 99(5) – giving a warning notice and election

Section 99(5) preserves the claimant’s freedom of action: giving a warning notice does not lock the claimant into court proceedings, and does not stop it from taking some different action instead. The election point was considered under the NSW analogue in Rojo Building Pty Ltd v Jillcris Pty Ltd [2007] NSWSC 880, where McDougall J held that simply serving a notice of intention to apply for adjudication did not amount to an election:

For present purposes, I am prepared to assume that s 15(2)(a) does provide for inconsistent alternatives, and that it does raise a question of election. That I think would be an election between remedies, not an election between rights. The right is to be paid the progress payment, or the statutory liability created by s 14(4). Section 15(2)(a) provides alternative remedies whereby that right can be enforced.

But, assuming that s 15(2)(a) does entail the notion of election, it is an election between bringing proceedings in a court and making an adjudication application. As I have said, a notice of intention to apply for adjudication does not amount to making an adjudication application. It is a procedural, although necessary, precondition to the making of such an application.

In those circumstances, I do not accept that the mere giving of a notice under s 17(2)(a) is of itself sufficient to constitute, or to trigger, the making of any election for which s 15(2)(a) provides.

…

The question in those cases arose in the context of election at law between alternative and inconsistent rights. For the reasons that I have given, I think that what is involved here is at most a question of election between alternative and inconsistent statutory remedies. The application by analogy of legal doctrines relating to election between inconsistent rights to statutory election between inconsistent remedies requires careful consideration. Specifically, it requires consideration of the extent to which the common law doctrine can sit with the statutory scheme. Since I have concluded that there was no election, it is unnecessary to pursue that topic further.

See also Rojo Building Pty Ltd v Jillcris Pty Ltd [2006] NSWSC 309. The result in Rojo sits comfortably with section 99(5), which expressly says that giving a warning notice neither commits the claimant to the action stated in the notice nor stops it from taking different action.

Section 100 – Proceedings to recover unpaid amount as debt

Where a claimant sues under section 78(2)(a) to recover an unpaid claim as a debt, section 100 turns the proceeding into a near-summary recovery: the respondent cannot run a counterclaim and cannot raise any defence in relation to matters arising under the construction contract.

Section 100 BIF Act – Proceedings to recover unpaid amount as debt
100 Proceedings to recover unpaid amount as debt

(1)  This section applies if a claimant starts proceedings in a court under section 78(2)(a) to recover an unpaid amount from a respondent as a debt owing to the claimant.

(2)  Judgment in favour of the claimant is not to be given by a court unless the court is satisfied that—

(a)  the respondent did not pay the amount to the claimant on or before the due date for the progress payment to which the payment claim relates; and

(b)  if the respondent’s liability to pay the amount arises because of a failure to give a payment schedule—the respondent did not give the claimant a payment schedule within the time required to do so under this Act.

(3)  The respondent is not, in those proceedings, entitled—

(a)  to bring any counterclaim against the claimant; or

(b)  to raise any defence in relation to matters arising under the construction contract.

Section 100(3) – the scope of the prohibition

The leading Queensland authority is Neumann Contractors Pty Ltd v Traspunt No 5 Pty Ltd [2010] QCA 119; [2011] 2 Qd R 114, where the Court of Appeal considered the equivalent BCIPA provision (then section 19(4), later 19(6)). Muir JA emphasised that the prohibition is targeted, not universal:

[i]t is essential, in my respectful opinion, to have regard to the words of s 19(4) in determining whether or not it prevents a defence being raised. It prohibits a respondent to a payment claim, who has failed to duly serve a payment schedule, from bringing a counterclaim against the claimant, or from raising “any defence in relation to matters arising under the construction contract”. The prohibition is not against the raising of any defence whatsoever.

…

In my view, s 19(4)(b)(ii) prohibits the raising of a defence only if it can fairly be described as one which relates to matters arising under the relevant construction contract. Counsel for Neumann argue, partly in reliance on observations in Brookhollow, that having regard to the width of the expression “in relation to”, there is a sufficient connection between Traspunt’s defence and matters arising under the contract. To my mind the connection is tenuous. I do not consider that a defence that the payment claim relied on by the claimant is virtually identical in all relevant respects to a previous one made by the claimant meets the description in s 19(4)(b)(ii), at least, where, as is the case here, the merits of the defence stand to be determined essentially by the comparison of one document with another.

Had the provision been intended to catch all defences having a connection with the construction contract, however remote, all words in s 19(4)(b)(ii) after “to raise any defence” would have been otiose: all payment claims are necessarily concerned with “matters arising under the construction contract”. The aim of the provision would appear to be to prevent respondents to claims relying on allegations that the moneys claimed are not owing for reasons referable to the terms of the construction contract and/or to the parties’ performance or failure to perform thereunder. If such matters are to be relied on as a defence to a payment claim, they must be included in a payment schedule. This approach to the construction of s 19(4)(b)(ii) is consistent with that taken by Basten JA in Bitannia Pty Ltd v Parkline Constructions Pty Ltd.

Applying that targeted reading, the courts have allowed certain non-contractual arguments to survive section 100(3). In Aalborg CSP A/S v Ottoway Engineering Pty Ltd [2017] SASFC 158, the Full Court of South Australia held that the equivalent provision did not preclude a respondent from running misleading or deceptive conduct or estoppel arguments to challenge whether a payment claim had been appropriately served — on the facts, Ottoway had served 22 successive payment claims by email and hard copy to Aalborg’s head office in Denmark, but its 23rd was served only by hard copy to the registered office in Australia.

By contrast, in Somerset Civil Pty Ltd v Eaglerise Developments Pty Ltd; Eaglerise Developments Pty Ltd v Somerset Civil Pty Ltd [2021] QDC 070, the Queensland District Court held that a respondent to a section 100 summary judgment proceeding cannot commence its own proceedings on the same issues and rely on contractual matters. The respondent had tried to start a separate proceeding alleging that the work was unlicensed and then asked to consolidate. The court refused, on the basis that joining the proceedings would defeat the purpose of section 100(3), which expressly bars a counterclaim or contract-based defence in the recovery proceeding.
